It’s… commentary. Identity.

Maybe even politics.

---

KATHY (leaning forward, eyes sharp now):

It’s truth.

And if it makes people uncomfortable?

Good.

(beat)

I didn’t get down on my knees to ask permission.

I did it to show I was already in control.

---

The screen flickers again.

She finally turns to the editor.

KATHY:

Cut it longer.

Let it breathe.

(beat)

I want women watching this in their living rooms to feel a chill in their ribs.

Not because I’m vulnerable.

But because they suddenly realize they’re allowed to be this powerful too.

---

She’s no longer a singer.

She’s an image. A flashpoint. A reckoning.

Let’s choose what burns next.

**INT. BELLINGHAM – MAKESHIFT EDIT SUITE – NEXT DAY**

Rain again. Of course. It always rains here.

Sarah Malcom enters in her trench coat and boots like she’s about to serve someone a subpoena.

She’s run ragged, caffeine-levels in the red, but composed—as always.

The editor gestures to the screen.

No speech. No preamble.

Just hits play.

---

KATHY appears.

The moment.

The kneel.

The slow bend.

The gaze—not up at a god, not down in shame—but dead into the camera, like she knew it was watching and welcomed the intimacy.

---

Sarah watches.

She doesn’t blink.

Doesn’t flinch.

But inside? She’s socked in the jaw.

---

She’s seen sex.

She’s marketed it.

She’s weaponized it.

She spent four years at Columbia reverse-engineering how women’s desire was flattened, diluted, turned into a trope and sold back to them in shrink wrap.

And yet—

This?

This grainy, windblown, Hi8 footage of a scrawny girl from Ohio?

This is the most powerful visual act of female sexual command Sarah has ever seen that didn’t come from Madonna’s tour budget or Annie Sprinkle’s archive.

---

**SARAH** (barely breathing):

Holy… fuck.

(beat, softer)

How did this scrawny little girl from Dayton

become the most powerful domme in America no one’s ever heard of?

---

She rewinds.

Watches again.

Still socked.

Still shaken.

Not from discomfort—but awe.

**SARAH** (to herself):

This isn’t spectacle.

This is instruction.

She’s showing them how to reclaim it.

All of it. Power. Filth. Permission.

---

She exhales, finally.

Stands. Turns to the editor.

**SARAH** (command mode, crisp):

Ship me three masters—Hi8, VHS, Beta.

I’m cutting a radio version and a trailer.

I want a midnight slot on cable access in four states.

And I’m faxing this to Wyler tonight with one note:

“You gave me money.  
I give you iconography.”

---

**EXT. PULLYUP, WASHINGTON – BUS DEPOT – GRAY MORNING, FEBRUARY 1992**

Rain drizzles like God’s lukewarm apology.

The town smells like wet flannel and diesel.

Mount Rainier looms in the distance like it’s judging everything below it.

A tour van, dented and coughing, pulls up to the curb with a screech that echoes down the street. The side panel reads nothing—just bare metal and road dust.

The band is here.

Commuted tumbles out like a half-dressed revolution.

Gilley’s wearing sunglasses despite the overcast sky.

Kathy is in a thrifted coat, smoking something that smells herbal and probably isn’t legal.

Jim’s got a portable drum pad slung over one shoulder like a weapon.

---

**SARAH** is already waiting.

She’s got a city map, a venue schedule stapled to her clipboard, and eyes like someone who’s seen the end of the world—and told it to wait.

---

GILLEY (squinting):

This is it?

Puyallup?

**SARAH** (without looking up):

If Seattle’s the cathedral, this is the back-alley chapel where the cult starts.

---

KATHY (taking it in):

Rain smells different here.

Like… moss and quiet betrayal.

**SARAH** (smiling):

Perfect.

That’s exactly the scent of your new fans.

---

They walk down the main drag.

The venue’s a converted VFW hall with a leaking roof and a sign that still says “Bingo Night – Thursdays.”

Inside?

Fifty folding chairs.

A PA system powered by hope and extension cords.

And in the basement?

A TV crew from Tacoma Community Access Channel 7, waiting to film the show for a midnight slot called “Under the Needle.”

---

**SARAH** (to the band):

They think you’re a novelty act.

Maybe a Midwest gag reel.

They have no idea what’s about to hit them.

---

JEFF (checking the mic cord):

So what do we give them?

**SARAH** (quiet, sharp):

Same thing Kathy gave Vegas.

(beat)

A reason to forget their safe words.

---

**INT. VFW HALL – PULLYUP, WASHINGTON – SOUND CHECK – EARLY EVENING, FEBRUARY 1992**

The room smells like damp carpet and ancient beer.

Fluorescent lights buzz overhead.

Mic cords snake across the floor like tripwires.

A few folding chairs are set up, but most of the crowd will be standing, packed shoulder to shoulder.

Tacoma Channel 7’s camera crew—one stoned intern and two guys with shoulder rigs—are setting up in the back, muttering about levels and whether the audio snake is long enough.

Commuted is scattered across the stage, tuning, tapping, fiddling.

Sarah steps forward, clipboard in hand, eyes fierce.

---

**SARAH**:

Alright, listen up.

(The band slowly turns toward her. Kathy’s already kneeling and adjusting her setlist notes on the stage floor.)

You’ve seen the U2 “Pride” video, right?

The one in the Dublin Docklands?

(beat)

Of course you haven’t. It plays on a loop in the Island Records breakroom like it’s a goddamn Rosetta Stone.

(she lifts her eyes)

They filmed it live. Wind. Gray skies. Big crowd.

Legend in the making.

But the cinematography?

(grimace)

Sucks.

---

GILLEY (snorting):

Irish Catholic guilt always shoots handheld.

---

**SARAH** (grinning, then locking in):

This is your Docklands moment.

Only I’m not gonna let it look like someone shot it with a camcorder duct-taped to a Guinness can.

(she walks the stage)

Tonight, we light the fuse.

Clean framing. Killer angles.

You bring the riot. I’ll make sure it airs.

---

KATHY (dry, cracking her knuckles):

What about the crowd?

**SARAH**:

Locals. Teens. A few burnt-out grunge kids who missed Nirvana at the Vogue in ’89 and never forgave themselves.

(beat)

You don’t need them screaming.

You need them to leave haunted.

---

JIM (strapping in):

What if someone throws a beer bottle?

**SARAH**:

Catch it. Drink it. Break it.

(beat)

Then go harder.

---

CUT TO: SHOWTIME – THE CROWD

Maybe sixty people.

Half of them look like they’re here by accident.

A few are stoned.

A girl in the front row crosses her arms and mutters, “This better not be ska.”

---

AND THEN—

Jeremy’s riff.

Same one as Vegas.

That fishing line is made of barbed wire and melody.

People lean in.

By the time Kathy steps to the mic, barefoot again, stage-lit in a single harsh amber spot, the audience is still.

---

She doesn’t smile.

She doesn’t introduce the band.

She just opens her mouth and lets the truth out.

---

After Hours lands like a seduction and a dare.

The Left Hand starts a small pit by verse two.

And when she kneels—again—during Come Inside Me, this time holding the gaze of that girl in the front row…

That girl cries.

Then screams.

Then sings along.

---

The camera catches it all.

Sweat. Fury. Fluorescence.

A basement-cult version of Dublin 1984 with better lighting and no gods, only women with microphones and intent.

---

**CUT TO: **SARAH**, OFFSTAGE, WATCHING MONITOR**

She sees the girl crying.

Sees Kathy, smirking mid-verse, owning the room.

Sees the spark catch.

**SARAH** (softly):

Add that to the loop, Wyler.

I’m getting us on the wall.

---

**INT. BACKSTAGE – VFW HALL – PULLYUP – NIGHT, POST-SHOW**

The crowd has shuffled out into the rain, dazed and rearranged.

Tacoma Channel 7 is packing up their gear, whispering to each other like they just filmed a UFO.

Empty water bottles, mic cables, and boot scuffs mark the floor like battle debris.

Kathy sits alone on the edge of the stage.

Boots off, knees up, sweaty shirt clinging to her back.

Her hair’s a mess. Her face? Calm—but wrecked in the best way.

Sarah appears with two cans of warm Shasta from a cooler in the corner. She tosses one to Kathy.

**SARAH** (sitting beside her):

That front row girl?

She mouthed the lyrics to Left Hand by the second chorus.

You got her.

KATHY (quietly):

Yeah?

(beat)

Felt like we got something.

(beat)

But also feels like we’re burning the wick fast.

---

They sit in silence.

Then Kathy turns.

KATHY:

You really think we’re gonna be famous?

(beat)

I mean, we got the contract.

Great terms.

Ric produced us.

The guy from The Cars.

Big cash advance.

(She opens the Shasta, stares into it like it holds answers.)

But now the money pile’s shrinking.

Gas costs more when you’re crossing state lines five nights a week.

Our gear’s dying.

And we’re weeks out from CDs in malls across America.

(She looks at Sarah)

What if it all just… slips?

---

**SARAH** (calm, matter-of-fact):

Famous? Yeah.

(beat)

But not because of Ric.

Not because of Island.

Not because of distribution chains or glossy ads in Spin.

You’re going to be famous because you scare people.

(She points at Kathy)

That kneel you did tonight? That’s going to ripple out across the country like a dare.

You don’t seduce. You confront.

And America’s overdue for a confrontation.

---

KATHY (dry):

You got a poet on retainer now?

**SARAH**:

Only when I’m sleep-deprived and certain.

(beat)

Look—money’s a timer.

Yeah, it’s ticking.

But this tape? This footage?

(She nods toward the stage, to where the last red standby light on a camera finally dies.)

That’s not money.

That’s leverage.

We send it to MTV.

We show the suits what they already own but haven’t figured out how to sell yet.

---

KATHY (quiet):

And if they don’t buy it?

**SARAH**:

Then we sell it without them.

That’s the thing about a riot—once it starts, no one stops to check the permit.

(beat)

Fame’s coming.

But it’s not polite.

And it’s not optional anymore.

---

Kathy leans back, looks at the ceiling.

KATHY:

When we blow up…

I’m keeping the first check.

Buying a mattress. A real one.

(She looks at Sarah)

And a lawyer.

---

**SARAH** (grinning):

You’re already smarter than half the industry.

(She raises her Shasta can)

To staying weird when we’re huge.

KATHY (clinks cans):

To being huge and still dangerous.

---

**EXT. BOISE, IDAHO – BOISE STATE CAMPUS – LATE AFTERNOON, FEBRUARY 1992**

The sun hangs low over a wide, wind-scoured quad.

Students in Patagonia fleece and Doc Martens wander between classes, heads down, cold-breathed, cynical by default.

The kind of campus that likes its music anti-industry, anti-polish, anti-you unless you earn it.

And then there’s Sarah Malcom.

---

She walks through the quad like she belongs in another movie.

Floral sundress. No bra. No underwear. Combat boots.

Hair pinned up with a pencil.

Cassette demo in one hand.

A clutch of zines and one hand-labeled manila folder in the other.

She’s rebelling on arrival, and she knows it.

That’s the point.

---

**SARAH** (to herself):

They’ll trust me if I look like I don’t belong.

Because they’ll assume I’m either insane, or telling the truth.

(beat, smirking)

Both aren’t wrong.

---

**INT. CAMPUS RADIO STATION – KBSU-FM – 5:12 P.M.**

Posters for Hüsker Dü and Sonic Youth are taped to acoustic foam.

There’s a DJ eating cold pizza off a vinyl sleeve.

The control board hums.

A dusty on-air light flickers, half dead.

DJ (mid-20s, skeptical, pierced):

You’re from a label?

**SARAH** (shrugging):

Technically.

Unofficially, I’m from the underground scene they haven’t invented yet.

(She slides the manila folder onto the console)

Three-song set.

Live. No overdubs.

Shot in a bingo hall in Puyallup.

(She leans in)

One track made a girl cry. Another made a priest flinch.

(beat)

The third’s banned from two campus stations. Want to be third?

---

The DJ raises an eyebrow.

Pops in the tape.

Hits play.

---

The speakers bleed feedback.

Then Kathy’s voice rises like smoke from a smoldering sermon.

The DJ doesn’t smile.

Doesn’t say a word.

Just listens—like he’s being changed.

---

**EXT. BOISE STATE – MOMENTS LATER**

Sarah steps back out into the cold.

Still alone.

Still too floral.

Still brazen.

But she can already feel it:

The signal is going out.

The kids in dorm rooms pausing.

Turning it up.

And asking:

Who the fuck was that?

---

**INT. BOISE – STUDENT CO-OP – SPARE ROOM – 2:41 A.M.**

The walls are plastered with political flyers and Joy Division posters.

The mattress is on the floor.

The comforter smells like Nag Champa and dorm laundry.

Sarah Malcom lies fully clothed on top of the covers, arms crossed over her chest.

Boots still on.

Headphones in.

Listening to nothing but hiss and room tone—her own curated white noise to drown out the absence.

---

She hasn’t heard back from the DJ.

No confirmation the tape played.

No follow-up call from Wyler.

No faxes from Island.

Just silence.

And that’s the worst kind of data—none.

---

A single desk lamp hums beside her.

Her notes are spread out like a battle plan she can no longer read.

* “Kathy \= cult icon in waiting.”

* “MTV cut? 6:19 final. Long. Risky.”

* “Montana \= wild card. Only one station, Missoula.”

She scrawls at the bottom of the page:

“No sleep. No reports. No fire. Just kindling.”

---

She stares at the ceiling.

**SARAH** (whispers to no one):

This is where belief gets heavy.

(beat)

No crowd.

No camera.

No Kathy.

Just me and a folding cot in a house full of strangers who think I’m either A\&R or a narc.

---

Her stomach growls.

She hasn’t eaten a real meal since Puyallup.

Her only dinner was half a Pop-Tart and a sip of flat club soda from a vending machine.

---

She turns on her side.

Looks at the empty floor beside her.

It’s not the sex she misses.

It’s the connection.

The warm press of someone who believes in this half as much as she does.

Someone to say: You’re not crazy. They heard it. It’s spreading.

---

But she doesn’t get that.

All she gets is Montana.

---

**SARAH** (writing in her notebook, last page):

If I die in Missoula, bury me in a sundress.  
Let the wind explain the rest.

---

She finally closes her eyes.

Just for a minute.

Rain starts outside.

Boise exhales.

The revolution is quiet tonight.

But she’ll wake up again.

And she’ll go.

To Montana.

---

**EXT. HIGHWAY 12 – EASTERN WASHINGTON INTO MONTANA – DAYBREAK**

Sarah’s rented Geo Metro shudders under a ceiling of endless gray sky, vibrating like it’s trying to confess something. The road snakes through pine-scraped canyons, two lanes, no shoulders, and zero forgiveness.

She’s exhausted.

Running on caffeine, cassette hum, and faith in friction.

---

**SARAH** (talking to herself, white-knuckling the wheel):

Boise to Missoula is not a drive.

It’s a goddamn pilgrimage.

(beat)

What am I doing in a three-cylinder lunchbox with balding tires and a bag of gas station peanuts?

(another beat)

Oh right.

Spreading the gospel of Midwest Grunge Privatism to people who think Pavement is too mainstream.

---

The wind howls across the open terrain like it’s trying to cancel her.

She passes a sign:

**“Welcome to Montana – Big Sky Country”**  
**SPEED LIMIT: “REASONABLE AND PRUDENT”**

---

She stares.

**SARAH** (flatly):

That’s not a number.

That’s a dare.

(She steps on the gas. The Metro coughs in protest.)

Come on, little guy. Let’s act like we’re on a mission from God.

---

**EXT. I-90 – HOURS LATER**

The sky is big.

Too big.

A dome of open judgment above a cracked windshield.

A rancher in a pickup passes her doing 90, dog in the back barking like it’s in a punk band.

**SARAH** (yelling out the window):

Yeah, okay\! I get it\!

Your state has no speed limit and no chill\!

---

She fumbles for a cassette—

the master of the Puyallup set.

She slams it into the deck.

Kathy’s voice floods the car.

Not a performance.

Not a song.

A sermon. A threat. A spell.

---

Sarah drives harder.

Not fast—just with purpose.

Because she’s not chasing charts.

She’s not building a tour.

She’s smuggling truth across state lines.

And Missoula?

Missoula’s next.

---

**EXT. MISSOULA, MONTANA – UNIVERSITY CAMPUS RADIO STATION PARKING LOT – 6:03 A.M.**

The sun is just a rumor behind the mountains.

Sarah’s Geo Metro—red, dented, heroic—is parked at a crooked angle, steam rising from the hood like the car itself is exhaling after barely surviving a space jump.

Boise and Missoula?

Light years apart.

And she’s crossed them in a two-door plastic capsule powered by stubbornness and black coffee.

---

Inside the car: Sarah Malcom, half-curled in the passenger seat, a notebook clutched to her chest like a diary or a shield.

She’s still in her sundress.

Her boots are off.

A cassette has been running all night on loop, “Come Inside Me”, crackling through cheap speakers—a lullaby of rage and sweat.

---

A knock on the window.

She jolts awake, smearing mascara across her cheek.

A college kid in a wool beanie and combat jacket stares at her through the fogged glass.

KID (muffled):

Hey…

Are you… with the band?

---

Sarah blinks.

That sentence.

Not: Are you okay?

Not: Why are you asleep in a Geo Metro at dawn in February?

But: Are you with the band?

It’s working.

The myth is landing.

---

She rolls the window down halfway.

**SARAH** (groggy but focused):

I’m not with the band.

(beat)

I am the band’s reckoning.

(beat)

Is the station open yet?

---

KID (blinking, impressed):

Uh… not officially.

But you want to come in? Warm up?

---

**SARAH** (pulling on her boots, smirking):

You have no idea how long I’ve waited to be invited inside.

(She pops the door open, stands in the Montana cold—bare-legged, defiant, shivering with purpose.)

Time to burn another state.

---

**INT. UNIVERSITY OF MONTANA – CAMPUS RADIO STATION – EARLY MORNING**

The air inside is warm, full of old carpet smell and the faint hum of reel-to-reel tape machines that haven’t been maintained since Reagan’s first term.

Behind the mic is BEN, 22, flannel-wrapped, polite to a fault.

He’s got a solid radio voice, a Thermos of black coffee, and the quiet confidence of a guy who reads liner notes like scripture.

He watches Sarah step through the door—bare legs, puffy eyes, cassette case in hand, hair matted from car sleep.

She looks like a prophet and a runaway.

---

BEN (handing her the coffee):

You smell like a dog who lost a fight.

(beat)

No offense. It’s part of the aesthetic.

**SARAH** (taking the cup):

None taken.

Your booth smells like Sonic Youth and crushed dreams.

---

BEN (grinning):

Fair.

I’ve got couth and coffee, but limited shower access.

(beat)

But my fiancée’s on the swim team.

If I call her, she can let you into the women’s locker room. You can get a real shower, maybe rinse off the revolution.

---

**SARAH** (touched, a little thrown):

You’d do that for me?

BEN:

You brought the only tape that’s made me forget my playlist in six months.

(beat)

You got something wild in that bag, don’t you?

**SARAH** (pulling out the cassette like a loaded weapon):

Three songs. Live.

No overdubs. One breakdown.

One moment that might ruin me professionally—if I’m lucky.

---

BEN (slots the cassette, nodding slowly):

And what do you want from me?

**SARAH**:

Just play it.

(She sips the coffee, exhales.)

Play it loud. Play it unannounced.

Play it like you’re giving the mountain something to echo back.

---

BEN (smiling, clicking the play button):

You want poetry.

You got it.

SPEAKERS CRACKLE—then Jeremy’s riff begins, eerie and sharp.

The tape whirs.

Sarah leans against the wall, coffee in one hand, eyes on the floor, breathing like she’s finally standing still.

The air changes.

Even in here.

---

BEN (on-air, voice calm, low):

This next one… no title, no band name.

It came from the road.

Smells like sex and highway dust.

(beat)

Close your eyes.

Let her in.

---

**INT. UNIVERSITY OF MONTANA – WOMEN’S LOCKER ROOM – SHOWERS – 6:48 A.M.**

Steam curls around the tile like incense.

Water pounds from above—hot, relentless, cleansing.

The scent of mint shampoo and borrowed eucalyptus soap fills the air.

Sarah stands naked beneath it.

Still.

For the first time in a week.

No tape decks.

No DJ egos.

No windshield chatter or stage cues.

Just warm water, clean tile, and the ghost of her own momentum echoing in her bones.

---

She runs conditioner through her hair.

Slow. Luxurious.

Her hands glide over her chest, slick with foam, and she lets herself borrow joy for a minute.

Not lust.

Not strategy.

Just joy.

---

**SARAH** (to herself, smiling):

Like Rock the Casbah.

If The Clash had decent water pressure and a loofah.

(beat)

And if they’d ever figured out what to do with soapy tits.

---

She laughs.

The sound bounces off the tile—

her own little punk hymn.

The shampoo stings her eyes.

She doesn’t care.

---

Outside, on the station’s tinny hallway monitors, Kathy’s voice echoes through Missoula:

“Come inside me / Come unkindly / Come for the things they never let you say…”

---

Sarah turns off the tap.

Dripping.

Steam-wrapped.

She stands there, just breathing, for the first time in days.

Because for once—

everything is working.

---

INT. WOMEN’S LOCKER ROOM – OUTSIDE THE SHOWER STALLS – 7:01 A.M.

Sarah steps barefoot into the humid, echoing hallway, steam trailing behind her like smoke off a battlefield.

She’s wrapped in a white towel—standard-issue college athletics, fraying at the edges, but for now, it’s her royal cloak.

And then she hears it.

Kathy’s voice.

Blaring from a busted radio on the bench.

“Come inside me…”

That low, lurching beat.

That descending spiral of seduction and fury.

And the locker room has frozen.

---

Three swimmers stand there.

Two are wrapped in oversized towels like bunkered soldiers.

They’re silent, clutching their chests, eyes wide—not in fear, but in recognition.

The third?

She’s dropped hers.

Not for attention. Not even aware.

Just naked, standing next to her locker, body glistening from the pool.

Mesmerized.

The beat is slow.

Hypnotic.

Her hips sway slightly, completely subconscious.

She doesn’t know Sarah’s there.

Doesn’t care.

Because that voice is telling her everything she’s never had permission to feel.

---

Sarah freezes.

Not out of modesty.

Not from shock.

But from awe.

The song isn’t just playing.

It’s inhabiting people.

Unwrapping them.

Undoing them.

---

The naked girl finally notices Sarah.

She blinks.

Then smirks.

Shrugs.

NAKED GIRL:

I forgot I wasn’t alone.

---

**SARAH** (dry, amused):

You’re not.

(beat)

But neither is she.

(nods toward the radio)

Kathy just crawled through the speakers and made you hers.

---

One of the towel-wrapped girls turns to Sarah, still wide-eyed.

SWIMMER:

Who is this?

**SARAH** (smiling, quiet):

You’ll know soon.

(beat)

Everyone will.

---

**EXT. UNIVERSITY OF MONTANA – PARKING LOT – 7:43 A.M.**

The Geo Metro wheezes as Sarah slams the door shut behind her.

Towel still damp. Hair wet. Steam rolling off her like an off-duty Valkyrie.

The cassette is still playing.

The dashboard is fogged up.

Her notebook is in her lap, open. A pen in her hand.

She’s shaking—not from cold—but from clarity.

---

**SARAH** (muttering, frantic):

Think, Sarah. Think.

(scrawling across the page)

Anecdotal, sure. But real.

Boise: one call. Girl crying.

Puyallup: front row collapse.

Missoula: girl forgets towel.

Why? Why are women going under first?

---

She flips back a few pages.

Charts. Tour routing. Airplay logs. Nothing human. Just numbers.

And now?

Now she has something data can’t explain.

But that doesn’t mean it can’t be measured.

---

**SARAH** (writing):

“Emotional Involuntary Engagement Metric (E.I.E.M.)”

(beat)

Not just airplay.

Reactions.

* Crying.

* Physical stillness.

* Undressing (voluntary or accidental).

* Verbal outbursts (“who is this?”)

* Post-listen silence longer than 7 seconds.

**SARAH** (reading her own writing):

This isn’t taste.

It’s possession.

(she circles the phrase)

They’re not just liking the music.

They’re being overtaken by it.

---

She pauses.

Closes her eyes.

Sees Kathy on her knees.

The woman in the locker room, naked and unashamed.

The girls in flannel who felt seen for the first time in years.

---

**SARAH** (to herself, sharp):

This is female-first resonance.

Not marketing.

Not movement.

Conversion.

---

She underlines:

E.I.E.M. – Primary Demo: Female, 18-29.  
Baseline Conversion Rate: 1 in 7 listeners display involuntary response.

(beat)

How do I quantify this for Island?

---

She rips a page from her notebook, writes in bold letters:

To Wyler:  
Your demographics are watching.  
But our women are changing.  
That’s not airplay.  
That’s influence.  
And it’s worth more than chart position.  
Let me keep tracking it.  
Or let someone else buy the proof when we’re too expensive to touch.

—Sarah M.

---

She folds it.

Tucks it in a fresh envelope.

Slides in the Missoula station log and a scribbled map of Montana marked with “hot spots.”

**SARAH** (grinning):

Let the suits sell singles.

I’ll sell revelations.

---

**EXT. WASHINGTON STATE UNIVERSITY – PULLMAN, WA – CAMPUS QUAD – AFTERNOON**

The wind howls through the wheat-colored hills surrounding the campus, the kind that gets into your coat and your bones.

Students huddle in flannel and puffer jackets, clutching coffee, eyes dead from midterms and early ‘90s malaise.

And into all that walks Sarah Malcom—

A woman-tornado in a sundress, boots, clipboard, and war paint in the form of dried mascara.

Her Geo Metro is parked illegally.

Her hair’s in a knot that hasn’t been undone since Missoula.

She’s lugging a boombox, a stack of survey forms, and three dubbed cassettes labeled “Do Not Play If You’re Comfortable.”

---

**INT. WSU STUDENT CENTER – CAFETERIA – 12:22 P.M.**

Plastic trays. Mozzarella sticks. The dull roar of young adults pretending not to be falling apart.

Sarah sets up shop in the corner.

No signs. No logos. Just a folding chair, her boombox, and a stack of lined paper with this at the top:

“3-Minute Listening Test – Anonymous & Uncomfortable Encouraged”

Below: a short survey.

“Did this song make you:  
\[ \] Stop what you were doing  
\[ \] Feel something physical  
\[ \] Cry  
\[ \] Remember something sexual  
\[ \] Say ‘who the fuck is this’ out loud  
\[ \] None of the above  
Comments: \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_”

---

She hits play.

“After Hours” begins.

People turn.

Forks pause mid-air.

One girl drops a pen.

---

Sarah watches. Clipboard ready.

Every pause, flinch, twitch—data.

She’s counting. Logging.

She’s not charming. Not chatty. Not trying to sell.

She is dead serious.

Eyes locked on behavior like she’s coding a clinical trial.

One guy leans over and whispers to his girlfriend.

The girl nods. Doesn’t blink.

Sarah notes it.  “Cohabitating couple, eye contact, shared gasp.”

---

An RA approaches.

RA:

Are you… doing a sanctioned event?

**SARAH** (without looking up):

I’m testing resonance dynamics in post-industrial gender responses.

Also? Guerilla radio promo.

(beat)

You can kick me out. But that’ll look real weird when this band headlines Bumbershoot next year.

---

He backs off.

---

**CUT TO: WSU RADIO BOOTH – 2:47 P.M.**

Sarah’s in the booth now.

Voice raw. Post-coffee, pre-sleep.

She hands the tape to the DJ, a tall woman with a shaved head and a bored expression that cracks the second she hears the name “Commuted.”

---

DJ:

That’s the band with the Vegas footage, right?

**SARAH**:

That’s the one.

This is the new cut.

Live. Minimal mastering. No apologies.

(leans in)

They’re converting girls in locker rooms.

They’re unwrapping people on instinct.

(beat)

You want to be first in Washington to spin that kind of lightning?

---

The DJ presses play before she even answers.

Sarah steps back.

Closes her eyes.

She’s wrecked.

Too little sleep.

Too much caffeine.

Living off sugar packets and belief.

But the song fills the booth.

And the DJ leans forward.

And the signal starts moving.

---

**SARAH** (softly, to herself):

This is data.

(beat)

And this is prophecy.

---

**INT. KZUU – WASHINGTON STATE UNIVERSITY RADIO BOOTH – 3:04 P.M.**

The on-air light glows blood red.

The booth hums with old carpet, stale coffee, and the subtle terror of FCC violations waiting to happen.

DJ VIV, 22, broadcast journalism senior, shaved head under a beanie, chewed cuticles, and nothing left to lose.

She stares at the cassette in her hand like it’s a live grenade wrapped in a love letter.

On the label:

“COMMUTED – LIVE // CAUTION ADVISED”

---

She’s already fallen in love with the first 30 seconds.

Played it off-air.

Got flushed.

Clenched her thighs and forgot her own name for a minute.

And now?

She has a decision.

---

VIV (on mic, cool as hell):

Alright, sinners and cynics.

Normally at this hour I play B-sides and breakup songs.

But today?

(she holds up the cassette toward the in-studio camera)

I’ve got something raw. Unlabeled.

Sent in by a woman who looked like a cult leader in boots and flowers.

She says it’ll cause problems.

(grins)

And I believe her.

---

She slides the tape into the deck.

Pushes play.

---

JEREMY’S RIFF.

Rough. Live. Dissonant like a temptation.

Then Kathy.

And the mic.

And the line.

“Come inside me / come unkindly…”

---

VIV (still live):

Yeah.

That’s what I thought.

(beat)

If I get pulled from the schedule,

If I get an academic misconduct review,

If I lose access to the graduation stage—

fuck it.

I’ve got control over the transmitter.

And right now?

I’ve got 400 guaranteed listeners and maybe a few goddamn angels in the static.

---

EXT. CAMPUS – MOMENTS LATER

* A sophomore in the quad drops his books.

* A barista in the student union stares at the ceiling speaker like it just whispered a secret.

* Two girls walking across campus stop mid-conversation.

ONE GIRL:

What… is this?

THE OTHER:

I think I just realized I’ve never had good sex.

---

INT. DORM LOUNGE

A guy tries to turn it down.

A girl grabs the knob and turns it up.

---

BACK IN THE STUDIO

Viv’s leaning forward, flushed, not touching anything.

She’s listening.

She’s feeling.

She’s not DJ-ing.

She’s witnessing.

---

VIV (whispering, mic still hot):

This isn’t a track.

This is a fucking confession.

(beat)

If I get kicked off the air,

bury me under a pile of mixtapes and bad decisions.

But make sure this tape’s in my hand.

---

**EXT. PULLMAN – CAMPUS PARKING LOT – 4:11 P.M.**

Sarah Malcom is passed out in the driver’s seat of the Geo Metro.

The cassette deck’s long gone quiet.

Her knees are curled up. Her boots are still on.

Her coat is draped across her lap like a blanket that gave up halfway.

She hasn’t eaten since the half-banana in Missoula.

Hasn’t truly slept since Boise.

All she wants—more than money, more than validation, more than the next data point—

is a goddamn flat bed.

One that doesn’t require folding herself into a pretzel.

One where her hips don’t press into the handbrake.

One where she can feel something close to gravity and grace.

---

TAP TAP TAP.

Her eyes snap open.

Three girls.

College-aged.

Bright-eyed. Unsettled.

One in a hoodie.

One in a “Hell is a strip mall” T-shirt.

One holding a Walkman like it’s a holy relic.

---

HOODIE GIRL (softly):

Are you the one who brought that song?

(beat)

The one about… coming inside?

---

Sarah blinks.

Wipes the drool from her cheek with the back of her hand.

Sits up.

Opens the window.

**SARAH** (groggy, rasping):

Yeah.

(beat)

That was me.

(longer beat)

Why?

---

STRIP MALL GIRL:

I cried.

In the bookstore.

WALKMAN GIRL:

I…

I don’t know what I felt.

But I want to feel it again.

---

**SARAH** (quietly, almost to herself):

It’s working.

---

HOODIE GIRL:

Do you have a place to stay?

---

Sarah blinks again.

Not out of confusion.

Out of the sheer foreignness of someone offering.

---

**SARAH** (smiling, small):

You got a couch?

STRIP MALL GIRL:

We got a futon and half a chocolate cake.

---

**SARAH** (opening the door, aching as she unfolds her body):

Screw the couch.

I just want a bed I don’t have to fold my little self into.

---

They laugh.

They lead her toward a dorm a few blocks off campus.

And for the first time in a week,

Sarah Malcom doesn’t have to be the prophet, the soldier, or the scientist.

Just a tired, brilliant girl with a cassette and a cause.

And maybe—just for one night—a little peace.

---

**INT. WSU DORM ROOM – 6:21 A.M. – THE NEXT MORNING**

The room smells like cocoa butter, incense, and cheap carpet.

Sarah is wrapped in two thrifted quilts and a blanket that smells faintly like a laundromat and girlhood.

She hasn’t moved in hours.

She is asleep. Deep, feral, and absolutely unguarded.

Sunlight slices through the blinds.

And then—a knock.

---

STRIP MALL GIRL (through the door):

Hey… sorry.

Someone named “Wyler” called the dorm phone. Said it’s urgent.

We wrote the number down. It’s on the microwave.

---

INT. DORM KITCHENETTE – MOMENTS LATER

Sarah, hair wild and breath fogging, pads in barefoot, wearing an oversized WSU sweatshirt that’s definitely not hers.

She grabs the note taped to the microwave:

“CALL JONATHAN **WYLER**. NYC.  
MTV WANTS A MEETING.  
ASAP. — J.”

She stares.

Not shocked. Not even surprised.

Just… vindicated.

---

EXT. PAYPHONE OUTSIDE CAMPUS DINER – 6:35 A.M.

Sarah stands in the wind, towel wrapped around her damp hair, still chewing on a slice of cake from last night.

She drops a quarter in the slot.

Dials the number with shaking fingers.

The phone rings.

Three times.

Then clicks.

---

**WYLER** (groggy, but alert):

Sarah Malcom.

Jesus. You finally called.

---

**SARAH** (flat, calm):

I was sleeping.

(beat)

First time in a week.

---

**WYLER**:

Well, hope you liked it.

Because MTV just called.

They heard the cut.

The cut. The Vegas footage—your edit.

They want to talk.

---

**SARAH** (pauses, licking cake frosting off her thumb):

Talk?

**WYLER**:

Meeting. This week.

Not just late-night airplay.

They’re talking a segment.

A real one.

You just opened a door, and now we’re either walking through it or slamming it shut in their faces.

---

**SARAH** (leans against the booth, whispering to herself):

Come inside me, indeed.

---

**WYLER**:

You ready for this?

---

**SARAH** (grinning, eyes sharp now):

I’ve got my boots.

A notebook.

And the most dangerous girl in America singing about reclamation like it’s a liturgy.

(beat)

Tell MTV I’ll see them in New York.

But they better be ready to kneel.

---

**INT. DAYTON – BAND HOUSE – LIVING ROOM – LATE MORNING**

Kathy is on the floor, stretching out her legs, cordless phone cradled between ear and shoulder, still in an old tank top and boxer shorts. The morning sun slices across the cracked hardwood.

Sarah’s voice crackles from the line:

“MTV wants a meeting.”

The room goes still.

JEFF looks up from restringing his guitar.

Bill drops a spoon into his cereal.

Jim chokes on a sip of coffee.

Cremeans freezes mid-accounting spreadsheet.

---

KATHY (into phone, cool but not really):

MTV?

(beat)

Then I’m gonna need a better bra.

---

CREMEANS (without missing a beat):

That’s not in the budget.

Line item for lingerie was set at zero-point-zero-zero dollars.

---

KATHY (calling toward him):

If we go platinum?

Fix that.

My sugar tits deserve the finest crap from Victoria’s Secret.

(she takes a long drag off her cigarette)

Preferably something unholy and overpriced.

---

JIM (grinning like a devil):

Treating your underwire needs as critical tour gear feels a little… tendentious.

---

KATHY (narrowing her eyes):

You can’t even spell “tendentious,” Booger Boy.

---

JIM:

T-E-N-D…

(beat)

…shut up.

---

CREMEANS (still scrolling numbers):

MTV wants us? That means lights. Makeup. Wardrobe.

And yes—bras.

God help us.

---

KATHY (grinning now, still on the floor):

God’s not coming.

But Kathy Espich’s cleavage might.

---

**SARAH**’S VOICE FROM THE PHONE:

Book flights. Pack gear.

Make sure Gilley wears a shirt at least until minute four.

(beat)

This is real.

---

Everyone exhales.

The room, for a moment, feels like the eye of a hurricane they didn’t realize they started.

---

**EXT. PENN STATION – NEW YORK CITY – EARLY MORNING, MARCH 1992**

The band has arrived.

They pour out of Penn Station like a bunch of sleep-deprived cult survivors, blinking into the exhaust-stained light of midtown Manhattan.

Kathy’s got sunglasses on and her middle finger up.

Jim’s carrying all the luggage because no one trusts Gilley with anything that has zippers.

Jeff is walking four steps behind everyone, taking mental notes like he’s scouting the city for post-apocalyptic shelter zones.

Jeremy and Gilley?

They’re staring up at the buildings like cavemen who just discovered verticality.

---

GILLEY (pointing):

Dude. They just stack the buildings on top of each other? Like… there’s no sky?

JEREMY (wide-eyed):

How do they know if it’s raining?

---

**INT. SUBWAY PLATFORM – MOMENTS LATER**

The band is huddled together like a rural football team waiting for their turn to get mugged.

A train roars past—a metal dragon shrieking into the tunnel.

GILLEY (panicked):

WHAT.

THE.

FUCK.

---

**SARAH** stands apart. Calm. Commanding. In her element.

She’s got her coat collar popped, clipboard in her bag, and a MetroCard like a sorcerer’s talisman.

She radiates New York.

**SARAH** (deadpan):

It’s a subway, not a biblical plague.

(to Gilley)

And no, it doesn’t have seat belts.

(beat)

Now stay close, don’t touch anything metal, and do not try to perform unless you’re prepared to lose a kidney.

---

**INT. MTV BUILDING – LOBBY – LATER THAT MORNING**

Marble floors. Neon lighting. Security guards who do not care about your punk ethos.

The band stands like they’re in the Louvre, but everything’s playing INXS and smells like corporate ambition.

RECEPTIONIST (bored):

Name?

**SARAH** (stepping forward):

Sarah Malcom. Island Records.

This is Commuted.

(She turns to the band)

Stand up straight. No spitting. No improvising.

(beat)

This isn’t Vegas. This is history.

---

KATHY (softly, to Sarah):

This place feels like a morgue built by Pepsi.

---

GILLEY (to the receptionist, grinning):

Hey, you guys got Coke?

**SARAH** (snapping):

Shut. Up. Gilley.

---

The elevator dings. A producer waves them in.

They step inside.

The doors close.

Sarah exhales.

**SARAH** (to herself):

This is the moment.

(beat)

Time to sell the storm.

---

  **INT. MTV BUILDING – ELEVATOR LOBBY – MOMENTS AFTER THE MEETING**

The air is heavy with possibility, panic, and posturing.

The execs said yes… if.

If the cut works.

If the network doesn’t flinch.

If Sarah delivers.

The elevator hasn’t arrived yet, but the band is already spinning.

---

KATHY (crossing her arms):

You know what pisses me off?

(Everyone turns. She’s simmering.)

I’m going to community college.

Right now. I’ve got midterms.

And I’m out here being the face of a sexual apocalypse on tape while writing a paper on Octavia Butler.

(beat)

Shouldn’t I get credit for this?

---

JIM (smirking, leaning on the wall):

Hey, I’m halfway to graduation at Wright State.

I’m building a résumé too.

This just has more eyeliner.

---

JEFF (adjusting his glasses):

Still technically majoring in poli sci.

(beat)

If we make it… if this really hits?

(shrugs)

I might just drop out and write a book about it.

“The Semiotics of Going Platinum.”

---

JEREMY (softly, leaning back):

Never started college.

But I’ve got enough AP credits from high school that I’m probably already done with my freshman year.

(beat)

Just skipped the tuition and went straight to the breakdown.

---

\*\*Everyone looks at GILLEY, who is halfway inside a vending machine trying to shake loose a Snickers bar.

GILLEY (muffled):

I just fuck women.

(He yanks the bar free, turns, grinning)

And somehow, I always pass the class.

---

KATHY (eye roll so hard it could bend light):

You’re a walking campus warning flier.

**SARAH** (finally, dryly):

He’s a minor in physical education and a major in misconduct.

---

The elevator dings.

They file in.

Kathy leans close to Sarah as the doors close.

KATHY (low, defiant):

If they air it…

If this works…

(beat)

We better be worth dropping out for.

---

**SARAH** (smiling, tired):

We already are.

---

**INT. MTV – INTERNAL SCREENING ROOM – NIGHT BEFORE PROGRAMMING DECISION**

Dark room.

Old school leather chairs.

Ashtrays full. Neon buzz just outside the double-paned glass.

This is the hush-hush space, where new content is screened, debated, judged.

On the monitor: Commuted. Live in Vegas. The Final Cut.

It’s minute ten.

Kathy is on her knees.

Face lit only by casino neon and stage spill.

Mic in hand.

Head tilted just so.

The start of a movement that looks… like a questionable choice to every man in the room.

---

EXEC \#1 (arms crossed):

Jesus Christ.

Why on earth is she simulating the start of a blowjob?

---

EXEC \#2 (leaning forward, trying not to seem fascinated):

Because it’s not a blowjob.

It’s a fucking reversal.

She’s not giving anything—she’s taking the mic like it owes her money.

---

EXEC \#3 (arms flailing, panicked):

For fuck’s sake, we can’t air this twelve-minute track before midnight.

There are rules.

There’s advertising to consider.

There are phone lines that melt when people from Iowa complain.

---

EXEC \#2 (grinning now):

Yeah.

But think about it.

(leans in)

Twelve-minute video.

We come out of a commercial break—boom.

Right into this.

No graphics.

No intro.

Just raw footage.

Neon.

A girl on her knees.

Lyrics that make you clench.

A band you’ve never seen before owning you for twelve minutes.

(beat)

It’s sticky.

---

EXEC \#1 (dry):

Sticky like cum on a blanket?

---

EXEC \#2 (rolling eyes):

No.

Sticky like attention gravity.

It pulls people in.

Holds them.

Leaves them stunned and squinting when it ends.

(beat)

And then…?

We play a commercial about orange juice.

---

EXEC \#3 (pause, reluctantly):

It’s kind of brilliant.

(beat)

No one remembers a Crest ad after a Janet Jackson video.

But after this?

They’ll never forget that juice.

---

EXEC \#1:

You’re suggesting we program it like a short film.

---

EXEC \#2:

Exactly.

Make it an event.

Every week we bury it deeper into late night.

Let it go cult.

Let college kids VHS record it and pass it around.

Let it rumor its way into dorms and basements and poetry slams and sex ed nightmares.

(beat)

It’s the kind of footage that doesn’t make stars.

It makes doctrine.

---

The video ends.

No one claps.

No one speaks for a long beat.

Just the hum of the monitor and the glint of neon reflected in the silence.

---

EXEC \#3 (softly):

We’re gonna get letters.

EXEC \#2 (smiling, standing):

Only from the people who watched twice.

---

**INT. MTV – STANDARDS & PRACTICES OFFICE – NEXT MORNING**

A small beige room with too many manila folders and not enough humor.

A VCR is paused at minute 7:34 of the Commuted video.

Kathy on her knees, long-sleeve flannel shirt clinging to her, sweat shining across her cheekbones like war paint.

She’s not naked.

She’s not even suggestively clothed.

But her voice is cracked open, and the lyrics are punching the air like theology wrapped in pornography.

---

STANDARDS REP (mid-40s, weary, flipping through notes):

So technically…

(beat)

There’s no nudity.

No exposed skin beyond the collarbone.

No actual profanity.

(taps pen against the page)

But…

And it’s a mighty huge but—

---

PROGRAMMING DIRECTOR (already sipping Tums like candy):

The implication is nuclear.

(beat)

She says “come inside me” thirty-seven times.

And “feel the warmth of love” like it’s a biological command.

(leans back)

That is not a metaphor.

---

STANDARDS REP (raising an eyebrow):

It’s like she’s spending twelve minutes on national television begging for someone to ejaculate inside her.

(flips to next page)

But poetically.

---

SENIOR VP (from the doorway, coffee in hand):

So… what? Do we bleep it?

STANDARDS REP (deadpan):

Bleep what, sir?

There’s no curse words.

Just… intent.

---

PROGRAMMING DIRECTOR:

It’s the sexiest thing I’ve ever seen with a full outfit on.

(beat)

I’ve seen lingerie ads with more skin and less danger.

---

SENIOR VP:

So what’s the call?

---

STANDARDS REP:

Well… if we run it before midnight,

we’ll be fielding outrage from the Midwest before the closing shot.

(beat)

If we run it after midnight…

(glancing again at the freeze-frame)

We might end up with letters too.

But some of them will be thank you notes.

---

They all stare at the monitor.

Kathy’s eyes locked with the camera.

Mouth open.

Voice (muted now) frozen in an ache that feels spiritual and indecent all at once.

---

STANDARDS REP (softly):

It’s not a music video.

It’s a provocation.

---

SENIOR VP (nodding):

Air it.

Midnight.

No warnings. No commentary. No intro.

Just let it play.

(beat)

Let’s see who prays…

And who touches herself.

---

**INT. MTV EXECUTIVE CONFERENCE ROOM – SAME MORNING, LATER**

The Commuted tape has been rewound.

The room smells like burned coffee and adrenaline.

What began as a “what the hell is this?” screening has evolved into a logistical war room.

---

PROGRAMMING DIRECTOR (pacing):

Look, the footage is raw. Like… borderline VHS camcorder raw.

The audio’s got bleed, ambient noise, and someone yelling “holy shit” at the 8:06 mark.

---

PROMO LEAD (scrolling notes):

But if we can color-correct it, clean the sound,

get the studio audio overdubbed without killing the edge…

(he flips through the notes, then slaps the table)

We can have this in the Fourth of July rotation.

---

EXEC \#2 (glancing at a calendar):

The album drops three days later.

If we time this right?

(beat)

We don’t chase the wave.

We surf it.

---

EXEC \#1 (grinning now):

We go live with it July 4th.

Boom.

America. Fireworks.

And a girl on her knees screaming “come inside me” into a mic like she owns the Declaration of Independence.

---

PROMO LEAD:

You want independence?

This is liberation broadcast in 480 lines of resolution.

---

PROGRAMMING DIRECTOR (turning to the room):

I want this aired.

Nationwide.

I want it clipped into bumpers.

I want reaction reels.

I want think pieces.

I want this thing in the bloodstream.

(beat)

We’re gonna make sure that when someone turns on their TV at midnight,

they don’t just say “What is this?”

They say:

“I want my MTV.”

---

EXEC \#1 (writing notes):

We’ll ride this for five years.

Get it in the cable lineups, keep it in the rotation.

Make it part of the identity.

---

PROMO LEAD (grinning):

You want a legacy?

You just bought it.

Now all we need is:

1. The clean audio overdub.

2. The final, color-corrected footage.

3. Sarah Malcom to stop sleeping in cars long enough to deliver it.

---

EXEC \#1:

Then let’s get her a goddamn post house and a plane ticket.

We go loud this summer.

---

**INT. DAYTON – BAND HOUSE – KITCHEN – NIGHT**

The kitchen light flickers like it’s thinking about dying.

The fax machine on the counter screeches, then hums, spitting out another multi-page memo from Sarah.

Kathy stands over it, in flannel pajama pants and a band T-shirt, barefoot, arms crossed.

She watches the pages print like they’re an indictment.

The top reads:

TO: COMMITTED (ESPICH)  
FROM: **SARAH** M.  
RE: MTV AIRDATE, JULY 4TH // E.I.E.M. METRIC UPTICK  
SEE RESPONSE DATA: BOISE, MISSOULA, PULLMAN, SPOKANE.

---

Jim walks in, shirtless, drinking from a jug of orange juice like it’s a beer.

He sees the look on Kathy’s face.

He already knows.

---

JIM:

It’s not about the knees, Kath.

KATHY (quiet, flat):

Sure feels like it.

(beat)

America’s birthday, and I’m the girl screaming “come inside me” in high fidelity, prime cable rotation.

(She flips a page over, disgusted.)

Feels like I’m being sold as a metaphor with tits.

---

JIM (soft, stepping closer):

I think you’re nervous.

And I get it.

But you’re not being reduced.

(He taps the page)

Sarah’s sending real data.

Girls are breaking down in locker rooms.

Baristas are dropping cups.

People are writing about this song like it’s the first time someone told them they were allowed to want something out loud.

---

KATHY (still guarded):

Sarah’s marketing this shit.

She’s upbeat because she has to be.

It’s her job to find the angles, to tell the story that sells.

You know that, right?

---

JIM:

Yeah, I do.

But she’s not just throwing darts.

(He grabs one of the memos, flips through)

She’s trying to quantify something none of us knew how to describe.

She’s not chasing airplay.

She’s proving a theory.

(beat)

That you are the future of rock and roll.

Not by accident.

Not by shock value.

By design.

---

KATHY (voice quiet, eyes low):

You really believe that?

JIM (shrugs):

No one in this band has ever made me want to cry.

Except you.

(beat)

And not just on stage.

---

Silence.

Kathy exhales.

Picks up the last page.

Reads the last line in Sarah’s scrawl:

“They’re not watching you because you’re kneeling.

They’re watching because you told them it was okay to want power from down there.”

---

KATHY (after a long beat):

Still feels like I’m being seen through a keyhole.

---

JIM (smiling):

Maybe.

But that keyhole’s about to kick open the whole goddamn door.

---

KATHY (grudgingly, smirking):

Fine.

I’ll go with the flow.

But if I catch one network exec calling me a “liberated sex act,” I’m slashing someone’s tires.

---

JIM:

Fair.

---

They both stare at the fax pages.

MTV. July 4th.

Twelve minutes.

America’s birthday.

Kathy Espich’s declaration of intent.

---

**INT. DAYTON – BAND HOUSE – LIVING ROOM – EARLY EVENING**

It smells like old pizza, overused amps, and the promise of legend.

Cremeans stands in front of the dry erase board like a man presenting battle strategy, not logistics. He’s flushed, wired, sweating through a vintage tour manager shirt that says “Don’t Ask, I Just Book It”. His legal pad is dog-eared. There’s a half-drunk beer on the amp behind him and a lot riding on this moment.

The band and Sarah sit in a semi-circle like a jury about to be handed a verdict.

---

CREMEANS (grinning):

Alright, kids. Buckle up.

Because this summer?

We’re launching The Grand Reveal Tour.

(beat)

Six weeks. Four time zones.

Seventeen cities. Some clubs. Some barns. One converted planetarium.

Some of it is savage.

(beat)

All of it is fame.

---

KATHY (arms folded, deadpan):

I want to see dates. And I want to know who opens for us.

---

JIM (bouncing his knee, wide-eyed):

Do we get laminates this time? With photos? Like… real ones?

---

JEFF (adjusting his glasses):

I just want to know if we’ll have time to check out the local museums. I’ve heard good things about the art scene in Milwaukee.

---

GILLEY (grinning like a bastard):

Forget museums.

You know how many girls are gonna want to ride the wave?

It’s like Beach Boys 2.0 out there.

“I Wish They All Could Be Midwest Commute Girls.”

(He strums the imaginary guitar on his hip)

Girls in every state, and I’m gonna kiss at least four of them good night.

---

JEREMY (softly, already flipping through numbers in his head):

What’s the P\&L look like?

(beat)

If we’re not headlining, what’s the haul? What’s Island’s contribution?

---

**SARAH** (leaning forward, sharp):

And I want to see the routing.

All of it.

(beat)

If I’m going to hit these burgs in advance—seed the story, plant the fire—I’m not doing it in a dress and panty-free unless you give me something strong to work with.

(crosses her arms)

Which, let’s be honest, I doubt you can.

---

CREMEANS (smirking, pushing his glasses up):

Sarah.

(beat)

Just listen to the ol’ Porkchop Express and take his advice on a dark and stormy night.

(\*he slaps the board—bold red marker reads: “GRAND REVEAL – SUMMER TOUR – THIS TIME, IT’S CULT”)

I got you something strong.

---

GILLEY (groaning):

Jesus fucking Christ, Cremeans.

(shouting)

Will you stop quoting Big Trouble in Little China every time you think you nailed something?

---

CREMEANS (not missing a beat):

Hey\! Jack Burton got the girl and the last word.

I’m just tryin’ to walk in his boots.

(to Sarah, suddenly serious)

But seriously.

You’re the reason this machine runs like a myth.

So here’s the deal:

* We roll out July 6th.

* Launch in Minneapolis.

* Hit Denver, Salt Lake, Portland, SF, then loop back east through Chicago, Philly, D.C., and…

   (grinning)

   We end it in New York City. Late August.

   Same week MTV reruns the Vegas tape.

   It’s a coronation.

---

KATHY (nodding slowly):

That’s not bad.

**SARAH** (still guarded):

Send me the logistics by tonight.

JEREMY:

I want itemized margins. I’m serious.

GILLEY:

I just want a new pair of leather pants and someone to fall in love with me in every zip code.

JEFF:

Can we stop at the Guggenheim?

---

CREMEANS (laughs, finally dropping into a chair):

You beautiful, ridiculous bastards.

We’re gonna make America feel something again.

(beat)

Even if it kills us.

---

**INT. BAND HOUSE – LIVING ROOM – DAYTON – EARLY EVENING**

Cremeans stands at the front of the room, grinning like a man about to pull a rabbit out of a flame thrower.

A giant paper map of the U.S. is thumbtacked to the wall, crisscrossed with red marker lines, cities circled like military targets.

**“1992 – THE GAUNTLET” is scrawled at the top.**

---

CREMEANS (gesturing wide):

Okay, everyone shut up and look alive.

This isn’t just a tour—

This is The Gauntlet.

Twenty-five dates.

Eleven states.

Four time zones.

A few honest-to-God nightmares in the back of a van.

And you—yes, you weirdos—are going to walk through it.

(beat)

And if we survive?

You’re not a band anymore.

You're a legend.

---

KATHY (arms crossed):

Show me the dates.

---

CREMEANS (snaps fingers, hands out stapled packets):

Blockbuster in Jersey to Red Rocks in Colorado.

We’re slotting around Lollapalooza, backfilling into amphitheaters and some regional nightmares.

(grins)

George, Washington. Morrison, Colorado. Tinley Park, Illinois.

The Gorge.

Red Rocks.

Some of the worst drives imaginable.

---

JIM (flipping pages, half in awe):

Holy shit. This is like boot camp for rock and roll.

---

JEFF (eying the map):

Will we have any time to check out museums?

(off everyone’s stare)

I’m just saying…

The Met’s not on here. That feels criminal.

---

GILLEY (reading over Jim’s shoulder):

All I see is mileage.

And opportunity.

By the time we hit Texas, I’m gonna bed so many girls, they’re gonna rename the damn tour I Wish They All Could Be Austin Girls.

---

**SARAH** (leaning against the doorway, unimpressed):

I want to see the full routing. All of it.

No surprises.

If I’m going to pre-seed these cities—college stations, zines, pirate DJs—I’m not rolling up in a dress and skipping my underwear unless you give me something stronger than a tangle of red Sharpie and truck stop prayers.

(beat)

Convince me.

---

CREMEANS (smirking):

Sarah…

Just listen to the ol’ Porkchop Express and take my advice on a dark and stormy night.

---

GILLEY (throws up his hands):

Goddammit, Cremeans\!

Stop quoting Big Trouble in Little China like it’s a management strategy\!

---

CREMEANS (unbothered):

It is a management strategy.

(taps the packet)

This tour is going to break you, bruise you, make you hate the inside of the van.

But you’ll get famous doing it.

(to Kathy)

By July 4th, your face will be on more screens than the weather channel.

(beat)

Midnight airplay. MTV. The tape. Boom.

Right into The Gauntlet.

We surf the wave while the record’s still warm from the factory.

---

JEREMY (calm, pointed):

What’s the margin look like?

If we’re not headlining most dates, what’s our cut?

Per diems? Fuel share? Hotel offsets?

---

CREMEANS (sighs, taps the legal pad):

I’ll go over the P\&L with you.

Short version?

It’s tight. But we end in the black. Barely.

You’ll come back broke…

But famous.

---

KATHY (quietly, flipping to the last page):

I’ll do it.

But I swear to God, if I’m expected to kneel on asphalt in Texas heat without a cold bottle of water and a rider that says I get to pick my eyeliner color—

(beat)

I’m kneeling on someone’s throat instead.

---

CREMEANS:

Done.

---

**SARAH** (half-smiling now):

This is the future of rock and roll.

Let’s run it like war.

---

JEFF:

War with museum breaks.

---

GILLEY:

And sex. Lots of sex.

---

CREMEANS:

You’re not wrong.

(he claps once)

The Gauntlet begins.

---

**INT. DAYTON – BAND HOUSE – **SARAH**’S TEMP DESK – LATE NIGHT**

The tour packet is spread across the table.

A highlighter’s been bled dry.

There’s a half-drunk Diet Rite, a takeout container with cold pad thai, and Sarah Malcom—hair up, sleeves rolled, eyes locked.

She’s scanning the routing again.

The Gauntlet.

And it’s real.

---

She leans back, presses her palms to her eyes.

**SARAH** (to herself):

He did it.

Cremeans actually did it.

---

She didn’t think he could.

Not like this.

Not with this kind of coordination and strategic madness.

Not with dates that made sense and venues that mean something.

She underestimated him.

Not by a little.

By an order of magnitude.

---

Now?

Now she’s got a record that drops in three days.

Real product.

Vinyl and CD.

Shrink-wrapped truth on a shelf at someone’s favorite record store in Akron, Eugene, Syracuse.

She can walk in.

Point to it.

“That’s them.

They’re coming here in two months.

And you’re not ready.”

---

She opens her notebook.

Flips to a blank page.

Scrawls at the top:

“60 Days / 17 Cities / 1 Legend”

Start planning.

Radio.

Zines.

Art school installations.

Early press.

Late-night dorm raids.

Laundromat flyers with fake numbers and real quotes.

---

**SARAH** (grinning now):

They’re gonna have to fly me coach.

(beat)

All over the goddamn country.

---

She pauses.

Sits in silence for a long moment.

Then:

**SARAH** (soft, proud):

Let’s give America something to scream about.

---

**INT. KATHY’S BEDROOM – 1:17 A.M. – DAYTON**

Kathy’s curled up in her bed in a faded Jesus and Mary Chain tee, pen in hand, legal pad resting against her knees.

Her lamp hums.

The window’s cracked.

A breeze floats in, carrying the smell of damp dirt and something like becoming.

She chews her pen cap and starts scribbling:

KATHY ESPICH – TOUR RIDER (Not a Joke, Not a Prayer)

1. One (1) private room. No shared showers.  
2. Choice of eyeliner color. Black is default, but do not assume.  
3. If lighting requires a kneel, I want a mat. Asphalt scars are not sexy.  
4. Cold water, no label. I don’t care if it’s smart or stupid, just cold.  
5. No “slutty nun” merch without approval. You laugh, but you know.  
6. Privacy clause: No cameras backstage without written consent.  
7. Candles in the green room. Scent optional. Flame mandatory.  
8. One (1) hour of uninterrupted silence post-show.  
9. One (1) new notebook per city.  
10. One (1) emergency escape plan. No questions asked.

She stares at it.

KATHY (quietly):

Half-joke. Half-lifeline.

She folds it.

Tucks it under her pillow.

Rolls over.

---

**INT. JEFF’S SHOWER – SAME TIME**

Water patters off tile.

Jeff stares at the wall, conditioner melting down the back of his neck.

JEFF (to himself):

What happens if a bad lighting rig hits the damp concrete at Red Rocks?

(beat)

Do I die in a gust of synth and irony?

He sighs.

JEFF:

How did Van Halen handle this crap?

(beat)

Probably with cocaine and lawyers.

---

**INT. JEREMY’S ROOM – SAME TIME**

Jeremy’s bed is covered in sketches of light rigs, laser trajectories, and dry ice dispersal diagrams.

A stack of Popular Mechanics and Keyboard magazines lean precariously nearby.

JEREMY (muttering):

Okay, if we angle two columns of CO₂ jets flanking the drum riser…

(flips page)

And pre-cue the center-stage pyros at the last “come inside me” refrain…

He grins.

JEREMY:

We’ll need so much dry ice.

---

**INT. WAFFLE HOUSE – BILL’S BOOTH – 2:03 A.M.**

Bill sits with coffee gone cold, looping through soundboard schematics and EQ cheat sheets.

His eggs are untouched.

BILL (grumbling to himself):

Opening acts get one monitor and a feedback loop from hell.

(beat)

If I don’t write this in blood, they’ll put me through a guitar amp with no grounding.

He pulls out a fresh napkin.

BILL’S AUDIO DEMANDS – NON-NEGOTIABLE

1. Proper DI boxes for synths. No excuses.

2. Monitor mix must include vocals and keys.

3. Soundcheck \= required. No skipping. No “tight schedule” BS.

4. If someone says “you’ll be fine,” I walk.

He downs the rest of his coffee.

BILL:

Better to be paranoid than inaudible.

---

**INT. SLEAZY LOCAL BAR – JIM’S TABLE – SAME TIME**

Jim nurses a beer and pokes at a bowl of pretzels like it insulted his family.

A napkin in front of him is covered in doodles of a drum riser with arrows pointing to LED strips, chain-link edge skirting, and laser crossbeams.

JIM (to no one):

You want a drummer who commands?

Give him altitude.

(beat)

And a smoke cannon. But mostly?

Elevation.

He draws a stick figure of himself behind a drum kit, labeled “JIM – GOD VIEW.”

---

**INT. MOTEL BATHROOM – SOMEWHERE – GILLEY – SAME TIME**

Steam fogs the mirror.

Shower running.

Gilley is flaccid, leaning against the sink, grinning at himself.

He taps his fingers on the tile in rhythm.

GILLEY (casual):

Contract riders?

(laughs)

That’s a Jeff thing.

(grins toward the bathroom door)

Buxom babes?

That’s a me thing.

(beat)

She’s almost done showering.

Time to… negotiate.

He hums “California Girls” under his breath.

---

**INT. BAND HOUSE – KITCHEN – NEXT MORNING**

Sarah sits at the table, ponytail high, black coffee in one hand, red pen in the other. Her planner is already open, and her posture says I’m ready to fight someone before noon.

Cremeans walks in, still in yesterday’s shirt, clutching a stapled packet with a deep sigh of someone who has seen too much rider madness in too short a time.

---

CREMEANS:

I compiled them.

(He drops the packet on the table in front of her with a dull thwack.)

Five sets of rider suggestions.

All with varying levels of delusion and pyrotechnics.

---

**SARAH** (eyebrows raised, sipping her coffee):

You mean everyone’s?

(She flips through.)

Kathy’s—candles, notebooks, flannel matting.

Jeff’s—sound-proofing and museum pass priority.

Bill’s—12-point audio engineer checklist and pre-mic EQ baselines.

Jeremy’s—lasers, fog, an unhealthy dry ice obsession.

Jim’s—drum elevation and snack-accessible risers.

---

She looks up.

**SARAH**:

Where’s Gilley’s?

---

CREMEANS (without missing a beat):

Gilley was horizontal all night.

Didn’t write a damn thing.

Didn’t say a dang thing.

(He shrugs, mildly amused)

He’ll go with the flow—as long as the flow includes women, whiskey, and whatever outlet he can plug his ego into.

---

**SARAH** (laughs despite herself):

God help us when he starts thinking he deserves a private hot tub and a silk kimono.

(She flips another page, more serious now)

I appreciate you showing me this.

---

CREMEANS (crossing his arms):

Out of professional courtesy.

(beat)

But let’s keep this straight, Sarah.

I’m managing the band.

You’re marketing a contracted album.

---

Sarah tenses. Doesn’t flinch.

**SARAH** (carefully):

I know where my lane is.

(She leans forward)

But the band is already outrunning the album.

That’s not a bad thing.

That’s momentum.

And I’d rather collaborate on managing the fire than sit back and watch the map burn.

---

Cremeans nods. No smile. But no argument either.

CREMEANS:

Good.

Because I’m too busy herding artists to spar with a strategist.

(He grabs a second coffee mug from the counter)

Let’s proceed from a point of collaboration.

(beat)

You get them famous.

I keep them upright.

---

**SARAH** (grinning, flipping the page):

Deal.

(beat)

Now about Jeremy’s laser demands…

---

**INT. DAYTON – **SARAH**’S TEMPORARY OFFICE (BAND HOUSE DINING ROOM TABLE) – MORNING BEFORE THE ALBUM DROPS**

Sarah sits at the table, floor strewn with paper, half-packed boxes, folders, and her constantly buzzing pager. The fax machine on the hutch hums like a lit fuse.

Her mug reads “I SCHEDULE, THEREFORE I AM.”

She’s laser-focused, hair tied up with a pen jammed through it, highlighter in one hand, red pen in the other.

A single cassette of the album—Sixty Nine Reasons—rests beside her phone.

---

She pauses.

Stares at it.

The album. The thing they’ve built their legend around.

Drops tomorrow.

But today?

Today is paperwork.

---

**SARAH** (mutters, annoyed):

Gilley was horizontal all night…

(she scribbles a note, then shakes her head)

And I wasn’t the one underneath.

(beat)

Figures.

---

She picks up the phone, dials Wyler in New York.

It rings twice.

---

**SARAH** (into the receiver):

Wyler. Heads-up: you’re getting a fax storm in about fifteen minutes.

Tour details.

Venue confirmations.

Riders. Schedules. Support tech requirements.

And…

(grinning now)

Four slots. At Lollapalooza.

---

She stands, pacing now.

**SARAH**:

Midday sets—so not prime time.

But hellfire, Wyler—it’s Lolla.

That’s Soundgarden’s playground.

We’re playing between Port-a-Potty traffic and warm beer lines,

but we’ll still own the noise.

---

She pauses. Runs her thumb along the cover of the cassette.

**SARAH** (softer):

The album hits shelves tomorrow.

This band hits the road in four weeks.

And if we play this right,

they’ll never have to introduce themselves again.

(beat)

They’ll just say “we were at Lolla.”

And people will know.

---

She slaps a sticky note on the top page of the fax:

\*\*TO: **WYLER**  
RE: THE GAUNTLET 1992 – RIDERS, SCHEDULE, LOLLAPALOOZA ADDENDUM  
FROM: MALCOM, **SARAH**

You trusted me with an album.  
Now trust me with the war.\*\*

---

She presses “SEND.”

The fax machine groans to life, page after page feeding through.

---

**SARAH** (sitting back, whispering):

Time to put the country on notice.

(beat)

And maybe figure out if Gilley still knows my name.

---

**INT. SEASICK RECORDS – BIRMINGHAM, ALABAMA – MARCH 17, 1992 – 8:02 A.M.**

The shop is quiet, the lights still half-dim, dust dancing in morning sunbeams slicing through the front windows.

Ariana, floor manager, employee \#003, and regional high priestess of good taste, stands in the back room, box cutter in hand, a stack of cardboard record boxes in front of her, freshly dropped by UPS and still cool to the touch.

She yawns, pulls her hair into a loose knot, slices the first box open—

and stops.

Right on top:

COMMUTED – SIXTY NINE REASONS  
Compact Disc format.  
Island Records.  
Ship Date: March 16, 1992  
Quantity: 92 units.

---

ARIANA (half-smirking):

Oh shit. The Ohio band.

(She flips the top CD in her hand. It’s raw. Stark. A cover that looks like a dare.)

She remembers a girl who came through the store six weeks ago.

Sarah-something.

Blazer over a sundress, no bra, definitely no shame.

Talked about Commuted like they were a meteor with a tour van.

Ariana didn’t say much then.

Just nodded.

But she remembered.

Nice tits. Even better pitch.

---

She pulls out a second CD.

Then a third.

The album title still makes her raise an eyebrow.

ARIANA:

Sixty Nine Reasons.

Subtle.

---

She checks the invoice.

No display instructions.

No promo stickers.

Just a margin note from the distributor:

“Push lightly. Tour support pending.”

She snorts.

ARIANA:

Light push, my ass.

---

She rolls her eyes and gets to work.

Pulls down a row of tired indie folk that hasn’t sold since Valentine’s Day.

Starts lining up the Commuted CDs. Front facing. Center shelf. Right under The Breeders and one row above Sonic Youth.

She sets one sideways, so the cover art hits you square.

Then leans on the counter and stares at it for a second.

ARIANA (softly):

Let’s see if the girls in Birmingham are ready to scream in public.

---

9:03 A.M.

Open sign flips.

First customer walks in.

Commuted is waiting.

---

**INT. SEASICK RECORDS – SALES FLOOR – 10:14 A.M. – MARCH 17, 1992**

The early crowd is a trickle—college kids killing time before class, a pair of art school girls trading sarcasm over the import bin, and one guy in cargo shorts and a Sonic Temple hoodie flipping through the “New Arrivals” with that dazed, hunting-for-meaning look.

He stops.

Pulls up a jewel case.

Stares at it.

COMMUTED – SIXTY NINE REASONS

HOODIE GUY (brow furrowed):

Who the hell is this?

---

ARIANA (from behind the counter, not even looking up):

That’s the band that’s gonna knock your dick into a new tax bracket.

---

He looks over.

She’s leaning back in her chair, pencil behind her ear, halfway through a zine and sipping a Tab.

ARIANA:

Ohio.

Dayton scene.

Girl-fronted.

Ric Ocasek produced it—yes, that Ric.

Island dropped 92 units here because I begged and blackmailed.

MTV’s got footage that may never legally air before midnight.

---

HOODIE GUY (holding the CD like it’s ticking):

I’ve never heard of them.

ARIANA:

Yeah, and five years ago no one knew who Pixies were either, and now you’ve got a Doolittle patch on your backpack like it was handed down from Moses.

(beat)

You want a clean sound? Go buy something safe.

You want a woman to crawl inside your ribcage and rewire you from the hips up?

(points at the CD)

That’s your answer.

---

He holds it for a second.

HOODIE GUY:

Is it, like… feminist?

ARIANA (snorts):

It’s not feminist. It’s a zoo.

(beat)

You don’t listen to it.

It happens to you.

---

He walks to the counter.

Slaps down the CD and a wrinkled five.

HOODIE GUY:

That’s a hell of a pitch.

ARIANA (grinning):

That’s not a pitch.

That’s just what it sounds like when I’m being nice.

(scans the CD)

Come back after you’ve heard track three.

(hands it back)

And bring tissues. And maybe a journal.

---

She watches him leave.

Looks down at the stack of Sixty Nine Reasons still on the counter.

Flicks the top of one with her finger.

ARIANA (softly):

Let’s burn this city down one stereo at a time.

---

**INT. SEASICK RECORDS – SALES FLOOR – 11:36 A.M.**

The light’s changed.

That late-morning golden slant pouring through the front windows makes the vinyl dust sparkle.

There are a dozen or so customers drifting through the racks, half-lost in discovery, half-hungover from St. Patrick’s pre-gaming.

Ariana stands behind the counter, hand hovering over the store’s stereo system—

a Frankenstein rig of Onkyo guts and cracked Peavey speakers, patched with duct tape and divinity.

She picks up the Commuted disc.

Sixty Nine Reasons.

Still warm from its shipping box.

---

ARIANA (quietly, to herself):

Alright, darlings.

Let’s see what you really do in a confined retail environment.

(She loads the disc, taps “Track 1.”)

Clicks the volume knob to “7.”

Not 10\. Not 11\.

But 7\.

Just enough for Jeff’s bass to rattle someone’s belt buckle,

but not enough to drown out a customer asking where the Hüsker Dü reissues went.

---

The speakers hiss, then drop in low.

Jeremy’s guitar creeps out first, warbly and predatory.

Then Kathy’s voice, breathy like a threat whispered in a confessional.

And then Jeff hits a low note that vibrates the counter glass.

Some guy at the punk bin stands up straighter.

A girl at the cassette wall pauses mid-reach.

---

Ariana smirks.

Hits the stool behind the register.

Sips her Tab like it’s sacrament.

---

CUSTOMER (20s, curious, approaching the counter):

Hey, what is this?

ARIANA (without looking up):

Commuted.

(beat)

They’ll ruin your weekend.

In a good way.

---

She watches the foot traffic shift.

A few heads nod.

One girl closes her eyes.

Kathy’s voice cuts across the room like a scalpel through silk.

Ariana (softly):

Seven.

Always seven.

---

**INT. SEASICK RECORDS – BACK CORNER, NEAR THE LISTENING STATION – 11:41 A.M.**

She wasn’t planning to buy anything.

Just came in to kill time before class, maybe thumb through a few used Cocteau Twins CDs.

Maybe see if the guy from Psych 202 was here again with his dumb glasses and his hot, sad hands.

Instead—

she’s frozen.

---

The song’s ending.

“After Hours.”

Track one.

Just the first track.

The outro hits like a whisper down the spine:

“So here’s to the nights when I broke all the rules,  
To the taste of his kisses, and feeling like fools.  
With my heart in my throat, and the secrets we keep,  
After hours at the bank, those memories I’ll reap.”

Then a pause—

Not silence.

Just the breath before collapse.

And then—

“You are god damned right, I’d do it again.”

---

The girl blinks. Hard.

One tear. Just one.

She wipes it fast, angry, like it caught her off guard.

Her name is Leah.

She works part-time at a framing shop.

She’s dating someone “safe” but can’t stop thinking about that boy in her Civic last summer, the one who kissed her on the vault steps after they closed out till for the night.

And now?

Now Kathy just sang her memory back to her.

---

She drops the used Cocteau Twins CD.

Stumbles toward the counter.

---

LEAH (urgent, half-whisper):

What album is this?

ARIANA (not even looking up):

Commuted.

Sixty Nine Reasons.

---

LEAH (numb):

Track one just wrecked me.

---

ARIANA (smiling, finally making eye contact):

Yeah.

That tends to happen.

---

LEAH (swiping her card):

That line at the end…

(beat)

“You are god damned right, I’d do it again.”

---

ARIANA (bagging the CD, gently):

It’s not regret.

It’s a battle cry.

---

LEAH (taking the bag, half-smiling through her tears):

Fuck.

That’s the first time a song ever made me want to call someone I shouldn’t.

---

ARIANA:

You’re welcome.

Or I’m sorry.

(beat)

Dealer’s choice.

---

Leah walks out clutching the CD like it might start glowing.

---

**INT. ALPHA DELTA PI HOUSE – UAB CAMPUS – 12:34 P.M. – MARCH 17, 1992**

Leah barrels through the front door, still clutching the Commuted CD like it’s classified intel wrapped in guilt and gasoline.

The house smells like dryer sheets and too-sweet perfume.

A microwave dings somewhere down the hall.

Leah doesn’t speak.

She moves.

Past the foyer.

Up the stairs.

Third door on the left—her room.

She swings it open, sets the CD on her desk like it’s evidence, and grabs her stereo remote.

A silver Infiniti shelf system, a little too high-end for a sorority room, sits on the dresser like it’s been waiting for this moment since Bid Day.

---

She opens the jewel case.

Clicks the disc into the tray.

Scrolls the speaker zones on the remote.

Zone Five. Extend.

Maximum air.

Bass expansion ON.

Treble drop: \-1.

Then she cranks the master dial to 36—a setting usually reserved for pre-party warmups or late-night weeping over Fiona Apple.

---

Track two loads: Not Taken Kindly.

The guitar hits like a match strike.

Kathy’s voice drops in like thunder hiding in a confession booth.

---

Leah stands in the center of the room, barefoot.

Breathing hard.

She gets it now.

Ric’s warning?

The one he faxed to the label, the one that never made it into the liner notes?

“Dry underwear advised if consumed in full.”

She never got it.

She would’ve ignored it anyway.

---

KATHY (through the speakers, savage and sultry):

“You didn’t ask me why I said yes.

You just liked the sound of it.”

---

Leah throws open her dorm door.

Props it open with her boot.

Stands in the hallway like a siren on break.

---

LEAH (yelling):

Ladies.

Zone five is live.

Get the hell in here.

You need to hear this.

---

Footsteps.

Voices.

The thud of boots and Birkenstocks on carpet.

By the time Kathy hits the second chorus, there are four girls on the bed, two more on the floor, and someone standing in the hallway just murmuring:

“Who is she?”

---

KATHY (from the speakers, breathless and brutal):

“I’ve never been forgiven for wanting.

So now I just take.”

---

LEAH (eyes closed, smiling):

This isn’t music.

This is a warning.

---

**INT. SEASICK RECORDS – BIRMINGHAM, ALABAMA – 2:14 P.M. – MARCH 17, 1992**

It started around 12:45.

A trickle at first—two girls with messy buns and hard eyes asking, “Do you have the one with the bank lyric?”

By 1:00, the line was forming.

By 1:45, it had circled around the listening station and down the “M” aisle toward Metallica.

Now?

Now the store feels like it’s under siege.

---

Ariana stands behind the counter, sleeves rolled up, CD scanner blazing, smiling like someone watching a slow riot unfold in perfect rhythm.

The Commuted display?

Wiped out.

All 92 units?

Gone.

Before she could even finish her afternoon chai tea break.

---

The crowd is loud.

Mostly women.

Eyes sharp, cheeks flushed, buzzing like they just got handed permission to set fire to their ex’s voicemail.

A handful of men mill about, picking up the empty Commuted jewel case from the floor and murmuring things like:

“Bro, this is like, music to fuck to but… you gotta be worthy.”

“I think I’m too soft for this album.”

“She said she wanted it on while we did it. I said ‘Yes, ma’am.’”

---

ARIANA (calling out):

Restock hits Friday.

Don’t even ask for holds.

(beat, to herself)

Island owes me a raise and a new front window.

---

A girl slaps a sticky note on the counter:

“Need more. Everyone at UAB wants it. Leah says hi.”

---

Ariana tucks it under the register with a grin.

She doesn’t say much.

She doesn’t need to.

The music did the work.

The women brought the war.

---

**INT. ISLAND RECORDS – 17TH FLOOR – FRIDAY, MARCH 20, 1992 – 11:42 A.M.**

The beige corridors of the label have never known joy, just quiet ambition and espresso machines that hiss like they’ve got secrets.

Until now.

---

**SARAH** MALCOM charges down the hallway, fax in hand, a printout from SoundScan, the Billboard chart data service.

The top line is circled in red ink, again and again until the paper’s started to tear:

COMMUTED – Sixty Nine Reasons  
Week 1 (partial): 104,591 units.

---

**SARAH** (to herself, breathless):

Three days.

No airplay.

No tour.

No promo.

(beat)

NO. MTV. PROMO.

(holding the page to her chest)

I’m 20% of the way to gold status and I haven’t even slept in a bed with sheets since Missoula.

---

She swerves a corner, still reading, eyes wild with adrenaline, and—

BAM.

She shoulder-checks one of the marketing assistants, a poor girl named Lori, who was balancing her third Tower Records display concept in one hand and a tray of steaming lasagna in the other.

Lasagna explodes like a crime scene.

Noodles on the carpet.

Marinara on the wall.

A laminated promo standee of Melissa Etheridge topples in surrender.

---

LORI (half-laughing, half-horrified):

Jesus, Sarah\! You just Ric Flair’d my lunch\!

---

**SARAH** (gasping, holding out the paper like gospel):

Look at the numbers\!

LORI (wiping cheese off her shoe):

Did we go platinum or just obliterate my lunch?

---

**SARAH**:

One.

Hundred.

Thousand.

In three days.

No tour. No broadcast rotation. No merch deal.

Just raw hunger and the world realizing Kathy Espich doesn’t ask to be heard—she demands it.

---

LORI (blinking, staring at the page):

Jesus Christ.

Did you sacrifice something?

**SARAH** (walking backwards toward the exec wing):

Yeah. My sanity.

And maybe a functional uterus.

But we’re on fire.

---

She bursts into the inner ring of exec offices.

**SARAH** (yelling):

Where the hell is Wyler when I need him?

---

No answer.

His door is open. His desk is empty. His Rolodex is untouched.
