She stands there, panting, hair falling out of her bun, marinara on her calf, gripping a partial-week Billboard report like it’s the Ark of the Covenant.

---

**SARAH** (to the office, the walls, the universe):

I need a budget.

I need a jet.

I need one intern who doesn’t faint at the word “orgasm.”

(beat)

And I need Camden.

---

She pulls out her notebook.

Scrawls at the top of a new page:

PHILLY: Make them kneel. Camden \= ground zero.

---

**INT. ISLAND RECORDS – 17TH FLOOR – OUTSIDE THE CORNER OFFICE – 12:29 P.M.**

The elevator dings.

Out steps Jonathan Wyler, Senior VP, champion of underdog acts when it suits him, and absolute master of the three-martini lunch.

He’s laughing with a pair of radio reps from Z100, adjusting his cufflinks, smelling faintly of dry gin, cedar, and just enough swagger to let you know he hasn’t paid for his own meal since 1984\.

---

**WYLER** (to his companions):

She’s a lunatic, yeah—but she’s my lunatic.

(beat)

Girl’s got more instinct than most execs have clean socks.

---

They pass the reception desk, still chuckling—until Wyler notices:

* A promo board knocked over.

* A smell in the air—Parmesan and panic.

* And Sarah’s planner on the floor, open, pen jammed into the spiral binding like a bayonet.  
---

**WYLER** (blinking):

Did we… have a food fight?

---

LORI (from behind the front desk, still dabbing her blazer):

She steamrolled me.

With lasagna and Billboard stats.

---

**WYLER** (pausing mid-step):

…What?

---

He walks slowly to his door.

It’s wide open.

And inside, he sees:

Sarah Malcom, standing at his desk, alone, flushed, sweating, hair half-fallen, a Billboard sales report in one hand and marinara streaked down her shin like war paint.

She doesn’t turn when he enters.

She’s mid-fax to MTV, Tower, SPIN, and at least one college radio syndicate.

---

**WYLER** (quietly):

She did it.

(beat)

Holy mother of God. She actually did it.

---

**SARAH** (turning slowly, fire in her eyes):

You want numbers?

(slaps the page on his desk)

104,591 units. Three days. No promo. No tour. No radio.

(beat)

And I’m about to make Camden the eye of the storm.

---

**WYLER** (grinning like a man who just found out his long shot is Secretariat):

You look insane.

**SARAH** (dead serious):

I am.

And we’re winning.

---

**WYLER**:

You’re covered in cheese.

**SARAH** (without hesitation):

I’m covered in victory.

---

He stares at the numbers again.

**WYLER** (softly):

This isn’t a blip.

This is a wave.

(beat)

Alright, General. You’ve got a budget.

You’ve got Camden.

You’ve got sixty days.

(He leans forward, eyes suddenly sharp)

Don’t lose the country.

---

**INT. KATHY’S BEDROOM – DAYTON – 3:22 P.M. – MARCH 20, 1992**

Kathy’s sitting cross-legged on her bed, guitar across her lap, idly picking through a new riff that sounds like heartbreak in combat boots.

The phone rings—landline, cord stretched across the room, a sticky note stuck to it that reads: “Only answer if it’s not Cremeans.”

She answers anyway.

---

KATHY (flat):

Espich.

---

**SARAH** (voice blazing from the other end):

Pack a bag.

---

KATHY (eyebrows up):

Excuse me?

---

**SARAH**:

We’re 20% to gold.

104,591 units moved in three days.

(beat)

Three.

Days.

With no airplay, no videos, no tour dates, no nothing.

This isn’t buzz, Kath.

This is a fucking seismic event with your name carved into the fault line.

---

KATHY (quiet now):

…damn.

---

**SARAH**:

Camden’s next.

We’re going in together.

Because why send one woman when two can make the ever-loving rubble bounce?

(beat)

I’ve got the strategy.

You’ve got the warhead voice.

---

KATHY (half-laughing):

You want me to play nice?

---

**SARAH** (dead serious):

No.

I want you to make them flinch and ask for more.

---

**SARAH** (rattling off, fast and surgical):

Philly’s not just a market.

It’s a gate.

* Six million people.

* Alt-rock heads.

* DIY punks.

* Jaded critics.

* South Jersey’s watching them.

* Bucks County is gonna go batshit.

(beat)

They once booed Santa Claus, Kathy.

If you can make them eat out of your hand?

The rest of the country won’t even blink.

---

KATHY (after a long pause):

So what’s the plan?

---

**SARAH**:

You and me.

Radio drop-ins.

Record store fly-bys.

Maybe one semi-illegal rooftop set in West Philly.

(grinning now)

I’ll be in a dress. You’ll be in flannel.

And by Monday morning, Commuted will be the reason college girls call their mothers and say,

“Don’t wait up. I have things to say now.”

---

KATHY (slow smile forming):

Send me the train time.

I’ll bring the boots.

(beat)

Let’s rattle the Liberty Bell.

---

**INT. PHILADELPHIA – ALT-WEEKLY OFFICE – SOUTH STREET – 5:03 P.M. – MARCH 20, 1992**

It’s Friday and the office smells like old newsprint, cold falafel, and deadlines dying slow, painful deaths.

Stacks of papers, crumpled cigarette packs, and unopened promo CDs clutter desks.

Someone’s playing Pavement too loud through a battered boom box.

Then a call comes in.

LINE EDITOR (picks up, irritated):

CityBeat. You’re late, I don’t care, what?

**SARAH** (on the other end, calm and deadly):

Sarah Malcom. Island Records.

Kathy Espich just arrived in Camden.

She’s not here to perform.

She’s here to invade.

You’ve got two days before she’s gone.

---

LINE EDITOR (sits up, scribbling):

Wait—Commuted?

The one with the track they’re calling “horny genocide on tape”?

---

**SARAH**:

That’s the one.

And she doesn’t care that you’re weekly.

She’s not waiting for your layout.

You want the story?

Run to keep up.

---

**INT. CITYBEAT BULLPEN – MOMENTS LATER**

The editor slams down the phone.

LINE EDITOR:

Alright, who wants blood?

(no one moves fast enough)

LINE EDITOR (shouts):

Someone find a writer who’s not afraid of women in boots and fury\!

---

**INT. RECORD STORE – LATER THAT NIGHT**

Kathy Espich is leaning against a record bin, signing a bootleg flyer someone made from a Xerox of the Vegas screenshot.

REPORTER (early 20s, flannel, wary):

Uh, hey, CityBeat sent me.

They said you’re doing some press?

KATHY (without turning):

You’re already behind.

---

REPORTER (pulling out a notebook):

Can I ask what you’re here to do?

(beat)

KATHY (turning slowly, dead serious):

I’m here to conquer.

(beat)

And I want to hear the lamentation of your women.

---

REPORTER (blinking):

Like… Conan?

KATHY:

No.

Like Kathy fucking Espich.

(beat)

Tell your readers to wear something washable.

---

**I**NT. CITYBEAT OFFICE – MONDAY MORNING, MARCH 23, 1992 – 8:57 A.M.

The office is already chaos.

The phones won’t stop ringing.

Someone spilled yerba mate on the copy machine.

The receptionist is fielding calls from three different student papers and a local feminist bookstore demanding advance copies.

And in the middle of it all stands RACHEL, junior music critic, flannel, Doc Martens, and a folder marked “Commuted: Field Report” under one arm and a look of utter disbelief on her face.

She storms into the editor’s office, slams the folder on the desk, and barks—

---

RACHEL:

CityBeat?

Are you even everloving insane?

You’re going to put this in print?

Have you read these lyrics?

(flips open a sheet)

This is track two—The Left Hand—and I quote:

“The world outside, it trembles as she sighs,  
Her skin a canvas painted with lies.  
Wrapped in crimson silk, she sheds her guise,  
A ravenous hunger lurks behind her eyes.”

---

RACHEL (waving it like it’s evidence):

What the everlovin’ hell, Ms. Espich?

What does this even mean?

---

LINE EDITOR (not even looking up):

It means print it.

Front page.

Bold pull quote.

(beat)

That track has already triggered two open mic nights, a midnight prayer group, and one senior thesis pivot.

---

RACHEL (lowering the sheet, incredulous):

It’s like… it’s like Sylvia Plath and a possessed lingerie model got stuck in a basement with a four-track and a bottle of wine.

LINE EDITOR (smiling):

Exactly.

---

RACHEL (still sputtering):

She’s not just singing, she’s summoning.

---

LINE EDITOR:

And Philly?

Philly’s listening.

(points to the board)

That track’s been played three times on WKDU since Thursday.

We don’t explain her.

We report the tremor and get the hell out of the way.

---

RACHEL (quiet now):

She scares me.

---

LINE EDITOR (grinning):

She should.

(beat)

That’s why we follow her.

Not to understand—

but to survive the orbit.

---

**INT. CITYBEAT OFFICE – EDITORIAL BULLPEN – SAME MORNING**

Rachel’s voice is rising.

Pages of lyrics are now spread across the editor’s desk like evidence in a high-profile criminal trial.

RACHEL (waving one of the lyric sheets):

No, no, no. This—this isn’t gothic poetry.

This is a masturbation metaphor.

Look at it\!

“She sheds her guise.”

“Hunger behind her eyes.”

“The world outside trembles as she sighs”?

Come on.

(points)

She’s not shedding silk, she’s shedding shame in front of a mirror and going to town.

---

LINE EDITOR (leaning forward, fired up):

It is not a masturbation metaphor.

It’s a rampaging female vampire metaphor.

A gothic seductress eating her way through the suburbs with velvet gloves and unapologetic appetite.

---

RACHEL (deadpan):

So… vampires now?

---

LINE EDITOR (dead serious):

Yes.

Emotional vampires.

Sexual vampires.

The kind that don’t ask permission and don’t send thank-you notes.

---

RACHEL (arms thrown up):

You think she’s a supernatural succubus,

I think she’s getting herself off with poetic flair—

either way,

we’re putting this on page one?\!

---

LINE EDITOR (grinning now, standing):

Exactly.

Because whether she’s biting necks or rubbing one out in defiance,

Kathy Espich just gave us the most electric, unsanitized, female-coded art since Liz Phair stole a 4-track.

(beat)

And Philly needs to see it.

---

RACHEL (pointing):

You write the vampire angle, I’m not putting my name on it.

---

LINE EDITOR (grabbing a pen):

Fine.

But you give me a pull quote.

---

RACHEL (reluctantly):

Alright.

(writes)

“Whether she’s devouring men or destroying shame,  
Espich makes it clear:  
this time, the hunger’s hers.”

---

LINE EDITOR (nodding):

Front page.

Above the fold.

---

RACHEL (quietly, as she picks up the sheet again):

Still sounds like she’s touching herself.

---

LINE EDITOR:

Good.

Let them figure it out after the shiver.

---

**INT. STAR 104.5 – PHILLY SUBURBS – LOBBY – MARCH 24, 1992 – 9:02 A.M.**

The sleek air-conditioned lobby of Star 104.5, Philly’s Hot AC giant, is bright, beige, and sterile—like a dentist’s office that plays Wilson Phillips.

Posters for Richard Marx and Annie Lennox line the walls.

Sarah Malcom walks in like an invasion in heels.

Floral sundress, tailored blazer, boots that shouldn’t work together—but absolutely do.

No bag. Just her notebook, her press folder, and the undeniable aura of a woman who’s been up since five and is already winning.

Kathy trails behind, in ripped jeans, sunglasses inside, and a flannel tied around her waist.

She looks Sarah up and down with that mix of affection and disbelief that only happens after you’ve toured a nation emotionally naked together.

---

KATHY (deadpan):

Why are you commando?

---

**SARAH** (without missing a beat):

Because it’s been my good luck charm since South Carolina.

(beat)

Also?

It feels good.

And dangerous.

---

KATHY (raising an eyebrow):

That’s your reasoning?

---

**SARAH** (turning, squaring up, full of fire and no apologies):

You want a promoter who feels feminine, good, and dangerous, right?

Someone who can walk into a hot AC station that thinks Alanis Morissette is risky,

and sell them you?

(beat)

Then stop judging my clothing—or lack thereof—

and let me be the woman you dare others to be.

---

Kathy pauses.

Stares at her for a long moment.

Then:

a crooked grin.

---

KATHY:

Goddamn.

You really are the sorority whisperer.

---

**SARAH** (adjusting her blazer):

And you’re the apocalypse they won’t see coming until it’s singing through their speakers.

(beat, nodding toward the studio door)

Let’s charm the suburbs, shall we?

---

Kathy walks beside her.

No more questions.

Just the sound of boots on tile.

Ready to shake a soft radio format to its core.

---

**INT. STAR 104.5 – STUDIO CONTROL BOOTH – 9:07 A.M. – MARCH 24, 1992**

Kevin, 24, board op, Temple dropout turned radio lifer, watches through the double-paned glass as Sarah and Kathy Espich settle into the studio.

Kathy drops into the guest chair like it owes her money.

Sarah crosses her legs like she’s negotiating a ceasefire in Milan.

Kevin’s hand trembles slightly as he adjusts the EQ.

Not because he’s nervous—but because he knows who they are.

The tape.

The Vegas footage.

The kneel.

He reaches for the landline faster than he’s ever moved in his life.

---

KEVIN (whispers):

Come on… come on…

---

**INT. WEST PHILLY APARTMENT – SAME TIME**

Angie, his girlfriend, is half-asleep in bed, surrounded by textbooks and a mostly-empty box of Honey Bunches of Oats.

The phone rings.

She gropes for it.

---

ANGIE (groggy):

If this isn’t about breakfast or sex, you’re already in trouble.

---

KEVIN (breathless):

They’re here.

---

ANGIE:

Who?

---

KEVIN:

Them.

Kathy fucking Espich and the bare-legged marketing witch from the Vegas tape.

They’re in the building.

---

ANGIE (fully awake now):

You’re lying.

---

KEVIN:

I’m watching her adjust her mic like she’s about to preach the downfall of Western civilization.

---

ANGIE (dead serious):

You have one job now.

Get me a goddamn autograph

or you sleep on the couch.

---

KEVIN:

You don’t even like CDs.

---

ANGIE:

I like revolutions.

And blunt women in flannel who don’t apologize for making boys cry.

You deliver and you might get lucky tonight.

But screw it up—

(beat)

And I’m blasting track three during sex with someone else.

---

KEVIN (saluting the phone):

Yes, ma’am.

---

He hangs up.

Stares back through the glass at Kathy and Sarah.

Kevin (to himself):

God help us all.

---

**INT. STAR 104.5 – STUDIO CONTROL BOOTH – 9:29 A.M.**

The interview just wrapped.

The host is still red-faced from trying to pivot out of Kathy’s impromptu monologue on lyrical ownership and orgasmic autonomy.

Sarah’s already halfway through negotiating three on-air drops and a future acoustic session on her own terms.

Kevin, sweating slightly, clutches a jewel case of Sixty Nine Reasons. It’s trembling a little in his hand. A black Sharpie dangles from his wrist on a string like a weapon he’s afraid to use.

He pushes into the studio like he’s not sure he’s allowed to breathe inside it.

---

KEVIN (quiet):

Ms. Espich?

(Kathy turns, sunglasses on indoors, sipping from the station’s sad styrofoam coffee cup.)

---

KATHY:

Let me guess.

You need me to spell your name right.

---

KEVIN (holding up the CD, sheepish):

Actually, it’s not for me.

(beat)

It’s for my girlfriend.

She said… well, I either come back with your signature or I sleep on the couch.

And maybe lose some… privileges.

(a little proud)

Also she called you “a feral Joan of Arc with better boots.”

---

KATHY (pausing mid-sip):

…okay.

(grinning)

I’ve signed a lot of stuff.

Bars. Jackets. Napkins. One guy’s shoulder blade.

But a radio station request?

(She takes the CD, cracks the case open, signs the insert with flair.)

“To Angie — Stay loud. Stay sharp. Stay on top.  
— K.E.”

She caps the pen and hands it back.

---

KATHY (smirking):

Is this gonna help you score tonight, Kevin?

---

KEVIN (dead serious):

Ma’am, I believe this just changed the outcome of my weekend.

---

KATHY:

Then go forth, blessed and lightly terrified.

---

Sarah (grinning from across the room):

You’re welcome, Kevin.

You’re part of the revolution now.

---

Kevin backs out like he’s just been knighted.

---

**INT. KEVIN’S CAR – STAR 104.5 PARKING LOT – 9:43 A.M.**

Kevin slams the door shut, fumbles with the keys, and hits speed dial on his brick-sized Motorola cellular like he’s defusing a bomb.

The Commuted CD sits on the passenger seat, still warm from Kathy’s hand, the liner notes now a sacred text inscribed with:

“To Angie — Stay loud. Stay sharp. Stay on top. — K.E.”

The phone rings once.

---

ANGIE (picks up, breathless):

Tell me you got it.

---

KEVIN (grinning like he just cheated death):

Got it.

Signed.

Personalized.

She even made a crack about me scoring.

---

ANGIE:

What’d she say?

---

KEVIN (mimicking Kathy’s voice poorly):

“Is this gonna help you score tonight, Kevin?”

---

There’s a pause on the other end.

Then:

ANGIE (softly, stunned):

She said your name.

---

KEVIN:

It’s real.

I watched her write it.

Sarah was there. Sunglasses. Boots.

Kathy was drinking shit coffee like she owned the whole frequency.

---

ANGIE:

You’re coming straight home.

(beat)

Put gas in the car.

Turn off your beeper.

And if you still have that flannel shirt I like?

Wear it.

---

KEVIN (half-laughing, half-panicking):

Copy that.

---

ANGIE (before hanging up):

And Kevin?

KEVIN:

Yeah?

ANGIE:

Don’t be late.

(click)

---

Kevin puts the phone down.

Looks at the CD.

Then himself in the rearview mirror.

KEVIN (to his reflection):

All hail Commuted.

---

**INT. ANGIE’S APARTMENT – WEST PHILLY – 10:07 A.M. – MARCH 24, 1992**

Sunlight filters through gauzy curtains.

A box fan hums in the window.

A single candle flickers on the dresser, half-melted but still hanging in there.

Angie stands in front of the stereo, still in her tank top and pajama shorts, barefoot on just-dried sheets that smell like lavender detergent and victory.

She’s holding the freshly signed Sixty Nine Reasons CD like it’s a holy relic.

---

ANGIE (to herself):

Signed.

Personalized.

And yes, sex is happening.

---

She pops open the CD tray, removes her original copy—the one she bought over the weekend when the girl at Seasick said “Track three might change your religion.”

She holds it for a moment, then places it carefully in a clean white envelope.

ANGIE (grinning):

You, my sweet, are going to Kara.

(beat)

After the main event.

---

She slips the signed copy into the player, presses play.

Track one. “After Hours.”

Jeremy’s guitar snakes in.

Jeff’s bass makes the window panes groan.

Kathy’s voice curls around the room like a smirk wearing eyeliner.

---

Angie turns to the mirror.

Picks up her hairbrush.

Mouths every single line.

She’s not performing.

She’s summoning.

---

She jumps on the bed.

The mattress squeaks like it knows what’s coming.

She smooths the sheets with practiced hands, then pulls the comforter tight.

ANGIE (singing with Kathy):

“You are god damned right, I’d do it again.”

---

DOOR BUZZER.

She doesn’t even flinch.

Grabs her flannel from the door hook.

Ties it around her waist, still barefoot.

Opens the door just wide enough to let Kevin in and her intentions out.

---

KEVIN (holding flowers like an idiot):

You changed the sheets?

---

ANGIE (dead serious):

I washed them in prophecy.

(beat)

You better live up to this album.

---

KEVIN:

No pressure.

---

She pulls him in by the flannel and kicks the door shut behind them.

---

INT. ANGIE’S BEDROOM – WEST PHILLY – 1:31 P.M. – MARCH 24, 1992

The fan spins slow.

The sheets are twisted, tangled, and half-draped over a bed that now smells like fresh laundry, skin, and victory.

Angie lies on her stomach, hair a halo of chaos, cheek pressed against the pillow, a pen in one hand and lined paper in the other.

Kevin is somewhere off-screen—probably showering, possibly passed out, definitely proposed.

Right between fuck one and lovemaking two.

She said yes before she even finished catching her breath.

---

ANGIE (writing, grinning like a girl who just outran gravity):

Dear Kathy,

I don’t know if fan mail reaches women like you.  
But on the off-chance this lands somewhere near your boots and bassline, I wanted to say—

Thank you.

I’ve had good sex. I’ve had bad sex.  
But today I had revolutionary sex.  
The kind where the sheets come clean and the soul goes dirty.

You gave me that.

I played track three loud.  
I screamed with you, not to you.  
And between fuck one and lovemaking two,  
my man—Kevin, the guy with the radio job and the nervous laugh—  
asked me to marry him.

And I said yes.  
Right there.

Because your music made me feel like the choice was mine.

That \*this time, I wasn’t just wanted—I was worthy.

I’m giving my extra copy to my best friend.  
Because I want her to feel like this.

And maybe you’ll never read this.  
But if you do?  
Know that in one apartment, in West Philly,  
a woman just chose her life.  
And you were the reason she knew she could.

(She signs it simply:)

With love and no apologies,

Angie

---

She folds the letter carefully.

Tucks it into the CD booklet of her original copy.

Labels the envelope: For Kathy Espich. Care of Island Records.

Then lies back and lets the moment linger.

Because music like that?

Doesn’t just sell.

It alters trajectory.

---

**INT. TEMPLE UNIVERSITY – TYLER SCHOOL OF ART – STUDIO B – 3:52 P.M. – MARCH 24, 1992**

It’s late afternoon in the sculpture studio—plaster dust in the air, rusted stools, and the unmistakable hum of a class that’s checked out mentally but still technically present.

The professor is glazing over a lecture about balance and brutalism.

Someone in the back is rolling a cigarette under the table.

And then?

Kara walks in.

---

She’s carrying a slim, white envelope like it contains a declaration of war and a love letter from the future.

On it is written:

“Use this responsibly. Or don’t.

Love, Angie.”

---

She pops open the CD player in the corner of the room—the one no one ever touches because it usually only plays Björk bootlegs or Velvet Underground live tracks.

In goes Sixty Nine Reasons.

She hits play.

---

Track one: “After Hours.”

It begins.

Soft.

Low.

Predatory.

Kara walks away from the stereo like she’s just set off an alarm and wants to see what survives.

---

Kathy’s voice slinks through the studio.

By the second verse, two girls stop molding clay mid-squeeze.

One boy lifts his pencil and doesn’t put it down.

The professor pauses mid-sentence, then blinks and just walks out. No explanation.

---

Track two: “The Left Hand.”

Someone whispers:

“What… what is this?”

Kara doesn’t answer.

She’s already peeling off her flannel and tying it around her waist.

It’s too hot now.

In the room. In her. In the air.

---

By the time Kathy reaches the bridge, two women from the printmaking program have wandered in from the next room, drawn by the sound like blood in the water.

---

One girl starts crying.

Another laughs so hard she drops a carving tool.

Someone turns to Kara and asks:

“Is this… allowed?”

Kara doesn’t answer.

She just nods.

---

By the end of track three, the classroom is silent, save for the CD spinning and breathing like a second pulse.

Someone in the corner finally says:

“Temple’s never gonna be the same.”

And they’re right.

---

**INT. TOWER RECORDS – PHILADELPHIA – MARCH 25, 1992 – 4:42 P.M.**

What was supposed to be a casual late-afternoon in-store signing has become a controlled riot with eyeliner.

The Tower Records storefront on South Street is overrun.

They planned for thirty fans.

They got three hundred—and they’re still coming.

Women in flannel.

Men looking overwhelmed but horny in a respectful, confused kind of way.

Girls in lipstick the color of bruises.

Boys who heard track three and haven’t slept right since.

---

**SARAH** and KATHY enter through the back door, security guard in front, a war general and her prophet.

Sarah’s in a fitted blazer, combat boots, and a Commuted button.

Kathy’s in her signature ripped jeans, long-sleeve flannel (open), tank top (black), smudged mascara, and zero patience.

They’re greeted by a store manager sweating through his headset mic and a pile of cassette dubs made by fans because Tower ran out of official stock yesterday.

---

MANAGER (frantic):

We had 200 copies. Gone in six hours.

People are trading dupes in the parking lot like currency.

---

**SARAH** (cool, stepping into the chaos):

Good. That means the market’s self-replicating.

---

**INT. TOWER SALES FLOOR – MINUTES LATER**

The moment Kathy steps out onto the makeshift stage, the crowd erupts—but not with squeals.

No.

This isn’t pop adoration.

This is religious recognition.

A girl holds up her flannel like it’s a flag.

Another holds a notebook that says:

“I wrote a short story after ‘The Left Hand.’ I’m not okay. Thank you.”

---

KATHY (to the mic, smirking):

You’re welcome.

Also?

You’ll be fine.

Or wrecked.

Hard to say.

Next.

---

The signing table becomes a confessional:

* A girl whispers, “My ex says I scare him now,” and Kathy signs her CD, “Good. Keep going.”

* A guy says he doesn’t understand the lyrics but feels “like I need to apologize to someone.”

* One woman hands over a cassette dub and asks Kathy to just bless it.

---

**SARAH** (running point, clipboard in hand):

We’ve got a photo line forming, three girls started a zine in the back corner, and someone just slipped me a note that says:

“Track three made me dump my fiancé. No regrets.”

(beat)

Also, the feminist bookstore across the street wants to know if they can get you for a poetry reading-slash-scream circle.\*

---

KATHY (signing an eyeliner pencil):

If they have snacks, I’m in.

---

The Tower staff are losing their minds.

They planned for Sharpies and selfies.

What they got was a flash-mob insurgency of rage, recognition, and \*\*very specific demands:

* “Will you sign my boots?”

* “Can I hug you if I promise to cry afterward?”

* “Will you take a Polaroid and burn it with me at the quad tonight?”

* “Will you sign my inner thigh?”

---

KATHY (pausing):

Are you serious?

---

FAN (dead serious):

I brought a Sharpie. And consent.

---

KATHY (to Sarah, grinning):

Best. Signing. Ever.

---

**INT. TOWER RECORDS – BACK OFFICE – PHILADELPHIA – 7:08 P.M. – MARCH 25, 1992**

The riot has barely cleared.

The air is thick with perfume, sweat, toner fumes, and something just a little feral.

The last stragglers are chanting lyrics in the parking lot like a street revival for girls who’ve seen too much and finally found a soundtrack for it.

---

MARK, the Tower Records store manager, is in his cramped back office, his tie loosened, headset jammed on, pacing like a man who just witnessed something he didn’t entirely survive.

He’s on the phone with Island’s East Coast Distribution Desk.

Other hand? Gripping a clipboard with an emergency restock request.

---

MARK (urgent, rattled, electric):

No. Listen to me.

You don’t have fans.

(beat)

You have a movement.

(beat)

They don’t want posters.

They don’t want pins.

They want blood.

---

He slams the clipboard onto the desk.

MARK:

I had girls signing each other’s throats in eyeliner\!

There was a Sharpie signed inner thigh.

My assistant manager got cry-hugged by five strangers and is rethinking her whole engagement.

---

He rips off a printed sales report.

MARK:

I had 92 units on Monday.

Gone by Tuesday.

People are taping the album and selling it for favors and zines\!

This is currency in a cultural insurgency.

---

MARK (dead serious):

I want 4,000 units.

By next Tuesday.

---

DISTRIBUTION REP (on the other end):

Four thou—Mark, that’s—

---

MARK:

I don’t care if you hijack a truck from Dayton.

Get it here.

(lowers voice)

This isn’t supply and demand.

This is ritual and requirement.

(beat)

The Left Hand is now a tattoo design.

If I don’t get restocked, my entire back wall is going to be taken over by a handmade Commuted altar.

(beat)

…and honestly, I don’t have the insurance for that.

---

**DISTRIBUTION REP:**

**We’ll move the inventory. I’ll push it up the chain.**

---

**MARK (sitting down finally, whispering):**

**Good.**

**(beat)**

**Because something’s happening.**

**And I don’t want to be the store that said no to the second coming of rock and roll.**

**(beat)**

**With boots and bite.**

---

**INT. PHILLY DINER – SOUTH STREET – 7:27 P.M. – MARCH 25, 1992**

The linoleum floor is chipped.

The coffee’s burnt.

The jukebox plays “Running Up That Hill” at just the right level to make everyone in the booths feel a little dramatic.

Sarah Malcom stands at the payphone by the bathrooms, Tower Records’ fax report clutched in one hand, a pen jammed behind her ear, and grease on her knuckles from ripping apart a pastrami sandwich she forgot to eat.

She’s dialing Wyler’s private line with the other hand, already smiling before the phone even rings.

---

**WYLER** (picking up, half-aloof):

This better be God or Malcom.

---

**SARAH** (laughing as it connects):

Close enough.

Tower just called.

---

**WYLER** (mock groaning):

What now?

---

**SARAH** (reading off the sheet):

They don’t have fans.

They have a movement.

(grinning, letting it land)

And Mark—their floor manager—wants four thousand units by Tuesday or he’s “gonna get steamrolled by a lipstick riot with a backbeat.”

(beat)

His words.

---

**WYLER** (dead silent for a second):

Jesus H. Christ.

---

**SARAH** (leaning into the booth wall, for once allowing herself to feel it):

I know.

(finally, letting it out)

I know.

(beat)

This thing we built?

It’s alive.

Not hype.

Not spin.

Not a PR stunt.

It’s happening.

(laughs, like something cracked open and light got in)

I just started a movement out of a maxi-single and a girl with combat boots and unresolved issues.

---

**WYLER**:

You magnificent bastard.

(beat)

How does it feel?

---

**SARAH** (smiling, letting herself feel it):

Like I’m not chasing the story anymore.

I am the story.

(beat)

And it’s writing me back.

---

Suddenly, from the corner booth:

KATHY (shouting over her half-eaten grilled cheese):

Are you done summoning the industry gods yet?

I wanna go do that slam poetry shit across the river.

They’ve got free mics and cheap whiskey.

Let’s go, lady\!

---

**SARAH** (back into the phone, still laughing):

Kathy wants to do slam poetry after supper.

---

**WYLER**:

Jesus.

Is Philly ready?

---

**SARAH** (grabbing her coat):

No.

(beat)

But they will be.

(click)

Dial tone.

---

She hangs up, shoves the fax in her coat, and joins Kathy at the booth.

---

KATHY (grinning, grabbing her leftover fries):

You get permission?

---

**SARAH**:

I got confirmation.

(beat)

Let’s go burn down a microphone stand.

---

**INT. CAMDEN – UNDERGROUND SLAM VENUE – 8:12 P.M. – MARCH 25, 1992**

It’s humid in the basement of this repurposed church.

Low lights. Folding chairs. DIY string lights tangled in the rafters.

The air smells like incense, weed, and secondhand revelation.

A girl named Ash is halfway through a piece about stealing back her own body through a stolen pair of Doc Martens.

Sarah leans against the back wall, arms folded, prepared for Kathy to take the stage and deliver a scorched-earth version of The Left Hand.

But Kathy’s not walking up with her usual half-smirk.

She’s got a rolled sheet of paper tucked in her back pocket.

And a new plan.

---

**SARAH** (whispering):

What are you doing?

---

KATHY (without turning):

Subverting expectations.

---

She steps up to the mic.

No intro.

No stance.

Just a voice. Cool. Measured. Velvet-gloved venom.

---

“Late night whispers, how I crave your touch…”

---

The crowd tilts forward.

It’s not the poem they expected.

This isn’t a scream or a slam.

This is lust with precision.

Desire with leverage.

Power delivered like gospel in electrostatic verse.

---

“Wrapped in crimson silk, she sheds her guise…”

Someone gasps.

A pen drops.

By the time Kathy says:

“Come up here, let me feel you inside me…”

The entire room is dead silent except for the hum of the mic and the tightening of fists in jacket sleeves.

---

She doesn’t shout.

She doesn’t posture.

She lets the words do the detonating.

---

By the outro:

“Feel the pulse, baby, feel it ignite…”

A girl in the front row is visibly shaking.

Two poets in the back have stopped pretending they’re too cool to care.

One boy covers his face and mutters a prayer he didn’t know he still remembered.

---

KATHY (finishing):

“…In this dance of heat, we’ll be alright.”

(beat)

You’re welcome.

---

She walks off.

No mic drop.

No grin.

Just leaves the silence behind her like a crime scene.

---

Back at the wall, Sarah is stunned.

Then smiling.

---

**SARAH**:

You detonated the entire room with a pop hit.

---

KATHY (grabbing a cigarette):

Damn right.

(lights it)

If I’m gonna be played on Hot AC stations,

I want them to remember why they’re sweating.

---

**INT. ISLAND RECORDS – **WYLER**’S OFFICE – NYC – MARCH 26, 1992 – 9:03 A.M.**

Wyler is mid-sip of black coffee, one shoe off, tie still in his pocket, flipping through his morning stack of memos when his phone rings.

He eyes the extension. Philly.

---

**WYLER** (answers, crisp):

Wyler.

---

PHILLY DJ (on the line, amped):

Did you know your girl just melted a slam poetry venue last night?

(beat)

I mean it. Like glassed it.

She read The Hilton like it was a threat and a love letter at the same time.

Girls were crying.

Dudes were sweating.

Some kid’s camcorder broke from sheer erotic force.

What the hell is she doing?

---

**WYLER** (pauses, leans back in his chair):

Yeah, so?

(cool, unaffected)

She’s Sarah Malcom’s girl.

---

PHILLY DJ:

Do you even know she’s pushing deep cuts into slam venues?

---

**WYLER**:

Nope.

(beat)

And I don’t care.

(leans forward, voice dropping)

Because she’s not breaking the plan.

She’s rewriting the map and burning the legend into unprintable corners of America while you and I drink coffee and try to keep up.

(smiles)

Let her cook.

---

PHILLY DJ:

You gonna ask her?

---

**WYLER**:

Tomorrow.

(hangs up)

After I greenlight another 100,000 units.

---

He slides The Gauntlet tour map back onto his desk.

Grins at the newest fan letter from a bookstore in Portland.

**WYLER** (to himself):

I don’t always know what she’s doing…

(beat)

But God help anyone who tries to stop her.

---

**INT. TEMPLE UNIVERSITY – WOMEN’S STUDIES CLASSROOM – ANDERSON HALL – MARCH 30, 1992 – 1:11 P.M.**

The seminar room is full—thirteen undergrads and a grad TA named Marla, who smokes clove cigarettes on the roof between lectures and never wears the same pair of boots twice.

The discussion topic was supposed to be “Sexual Agency in 20th Century Female Poetics.”

What they get instead is a grainy, bootleg camcorder tape, handed off like contraband by a printmaking major who mumbled, “Just… watch it. It’s her.”

---

The lights dim.

The tape hisses.

And suddenly—

There’s Kathy Espich.

On a low stage.

Low lighting.

Loose flannel and a voice like a fuse.

---

“Late night whispers, how I crave your touch…”

---

The classroom goes quiet.

Real quiet.

Like “no one wants to breathe too loudly” quiet.

No background music.

No synths.

Just words.

And Kathy’s voice.

Low.

Precise.

Undeniably intimate.

---

By the first pre-chorus, a girl in the front has uncrossed and re-crossed her legs twice.

By the bridge—“Come up here, let me feel you inside me”—someone drops their pen.

Marla hits pause.

---

MARLA (calm, cool):

Reactions?

---

SOPHIE (cross-armed, furious):

This is porn.

It’s exhibitionism dressed up as empowerment.

---

LANA (quiet):

No.

It’s control.

She owns it.

Every word.

Every gesture.

She’s not asking for desire.

She’s orchestrating it.

---

JESS (almost whispering):

It made me… uncomfortable.

(beat)

But seen.

---

RAQUEL (furious, eyes bright):

This is why we’re never taken seriously.

Because this is what they think we want to scream into a mic.

---

MARLA (raising an eyebrow):

But isn’t it?

(beat)

What if Kathy Espich is not speaking for women—

but just one woman?

And that’s enough?

---

DANIELLE (in the back, stunned):

I didn’t think I was allowed to say things like that out loud.

---

MARLA (smiling, hitting play again):

Apparently, Kathy didn’t ask for permission.

---

By the final chorus, four women are crying.

Two are visibly angry.

One is shaking like she’s just realized something she can’t un-realize.

---

The tape ends.

Kathy looks up.

Straight into the camera.

No wink. No smile. Just knowing.

---

The room stays dead silent for forty-five full seconds.

Then—

MARLA (softly):

Okay.

Now let’s actually talk about sexual agency.

---

**INT. MOTEL ROOM – ST. LOUIS, MISSOURI – APRIL 3, 1992 – 10:46 P.M.**

The TV is on mute, playing a local news loop about construction delays and baseball scores.

Kathy sits on the edge of a lumpy motel bed, half in stage makeup, towel around her shoulders, smoke curling from a barely lit cigarette. Her boots are still on.

Sarah’s asleep across the room, tangled in a blanket, a clipboard under one arm.

Kathy flips through the day’s collected fan mail and press clippings, barely reading until one envelope catches her eye.

Typed.

No return address.

Temple University postmark.

She opens it.

---

**LETTER (handwritten):**

Ms. Espich,  
My name is Marla. I’m a graduate TA in the Women’s Studies Department at Temple. Last week, someone slipped me a bootleg tape of your slam reading of “The Hilton.” I played it for my 300-level seminar class. We were covering contemporary female poetics and sexual agency.  
They were ready to coast through it. They had the answers already. They’d read the right books, memorized the buzzwords.  
Then you spoke.

---

Kathy pauses.

Lights the cigarette again, slower this time.

---

You shattered consensus.  
They turned on each other like wolves. They cried. They screamed. They clutched their knees and questioned their politics. You made a room full of third-wave feminist scholars realize they were still terrified of a woman who talks about sex unapologetically and dares people to watch.  
You did what two semesters of syllabus never could:  
You made them think for themselves.

---

Kathy exhales hard.

Stares at the paper.

The letter continues.

---

I don’t know if you’ll ever read this.  
But if you do—know that your words aren’t just being heard.  
They’re being argued about.  
Which is better.  
Consensus is death. You gave them fire.  
Thank you for the discomfort,  
Marla S.  
Temple University, Philadelphia

---

Kathy folds the letter.

Tucks it into her lyric notebook, the one with crossed-out verses and unfinished seductions.

She lies back on the bed, arms behind her head, staring at the ceiling.

KATHY (to herself, soft):

You’re welcome.

(beat)

Keep watching.

---

**INT. ST. LOUIS MOTEL – MOTEL ROOM – 11:24 P.M. – APRIL 3, 1992**

The lights are low.

Sarah’s half-reclined in the corner armchair, motel blanket draped around her shoulders like a general’s cloak.

Kathy’s still in her towel, now perched on the windowsill with her boots dangling, smoking with casual menace.

The room smells like heat, eyeliner, and old smoke.

---

KATHY (exhaling):

Thank god our connector to Kansas City leaves at a reasonable hour.

(beat)

What is it again?

---

**SARAH** (checking her clipboard without looking up):

Seven-oh-five.

Terminal C. Gate 18\.

Midwest Connect.

(grins)

They fly a plane that smells like vending machine coffee and indecision.

---

KATHY (softly):

Sounds like home.

(pause)

You got new sales data?

---

**SARAH** (instantly alert):

Hot off the fax.

Thirty-six thousand units this week alone.

We’re tracking mid-chart on the Billboard 200\.

You’re outselling half the dudes you’ve fantasized about killing in your songs.

(beat, grinning)

Island’s scrambling to keep up. You’re their accidental supernova.

---

KATHY:

Good.

Means I might get enough leverage to ask for a real hotel next time.

(beat)

Also… I want to see my mom.

Just for a minute.

(stares out the window)

She watches MTV now.

Says she hasn’t heard me sing since I was seventeen and screaming at the microwave.

---

**SARAH** (nodding, quieter):

I can get you home after Kansas City.

Maybe loop it between Tulsa and Dallas.

(then, carefully)

What about bringing Gilley out?

---

KATHY (smirking):

To meet my mom?

---

**SARAH** (laughs, then falters, then laughs again):

No—god, no.

Just… for Texas. And Oklahoma.

He’s got pull.

(beat)

Dudes in Amarillo won’t know what hit them.

---

KATHY (watching her):

You just want the charisma.

---

**SARAH** (shrugging, pretending to study her planner):

I want the heat.

---

KATHY (eyeing her carefully):

He’s got the hots for you.

---

Sarah pauses.

A beat.

Then a tight, controlled grin.

**SARAH**:

Yeah.

(softly)

And no one can ever know.

---

KATHY (laughs)

Secret’s safe with me.

(beat)

You’re both crazy, but it works.

---

They sit there for a long moment.

Two women in the aftermath of flames they set themselves.

Planning the next city.

Next rupture.

Next revival.

---

KATHY:

Texas and Oklahoma?

Let’s grind it.

Hard.

---

**SARAH**:

Deal.

(beat)

But you’re telling Gilley.

---

KATHY (grinning):

Fine.

I’ll tell him to bring his charm.

And a condom.

---

They both laugh.

The kind of laugh that only happens when war is going your way and you’ve still got miles to go.

---

**INT. ST. LOUIS MOTEL – MOTEL ROOM – 11:24 P.M. – APRIL 3, 1992**

A single lamp flickers by the bedside.

The TV is playing muted static in the background, the forgotten end of a local news broadcast giving way to darkness.

The room is tired—same as the two women in it.

Sarah’s curled up in the armchair, blanket around her shoulders, papers tucked under one leg, pen tapping rhythmically on her knee.

Kathy’s by the window, legs pulled up on the sill, cigarette barely lit, staring out at nothing in particular.

---

KATHY (quietly):

Thank God we fly out to Kansas City at a normal hour.

(beat)

Seven A.M. connector from Terminal C, right?

---

**SARAH** (half-asleep, not looking up):

Yep. Midwest Connect.

We should be wheels-up before we finish the airport coffee regret.

---

KATHY (sighing):

Remind me again—sales?

---

**SARAH** (instantly awake):

New SoundScan drop hit tonight.

(pulls out a sheet from the folder beside her)

We cleared 36,000 units since Monday.

That’s in addition to last week’s 104K.

(smiles softly)

You’re not climbing the Billboard 200, Kathy. You’re storming it.

---

KATHY (sits back, exhaling):

Jesus.

(beat)

You think we’ve got time after Kansas City?

---

**SARAH** (watching her):

Time for what?

---

KATHY:

Home.

(beat)

To see my mom.

I don’t know.

Just… something about seeing our name in windows across the Midwest makes me wanna stand in her kitchen and ask her if she gets it yet.

(smirks)

She always thought I’d marry a youth pastor and become a school librarian.

---

**SARAH** (laughs):

Yeah, well. Maybe if the youth pastor had a drum kit and a smirk problem.

---

KATHY:

Speaking of smirk problems…

(beat)

We bringing Gilley out for Texas and Oklahoma?

---

**SARAH** (quickly):

We’ll need him.

(flipping to another page)

He works in markets like that. Too much swagger, but… that kind of energy works on guys in boots and girls with rebellion in their veins.

(carefully)

Plus, he’s got reach. He’s sticky in radio. People remember him.

---

KATHY (grinning):

Sticky.

Nice.

---

**SARAH** (dryly):

Marketing term. Get your mind out of the gutter.

---

KATHY (stretches, tired):

Alright. Call him.

Tell him to bring that stupid jacket he thinks makes him look like a cowboy and prepare to make people sweat.

---

**SARAH** (nods):

Will do.

(beat)

Texas and Oklahoma—we go hard.

No apologies.

---

KATHY (climbing into bed):

No regrets.

(beat)

And no delays.

---

Sarah watches her settle in.

Kathy knows Gilley has charm.

She knows he works crowds.

She knows he’s reckless and useful.

But what Kathy doesn’t know—

—what no one knows—

—is that Sarah knows Gilley differently.

Privately.

Viscerally.

But it stays locked behind her clipboard, her calm, and her control.

Because it has to.

Because this isn’t about love—

It’s about power.

---

Sarah turns out the lamp.

**SARAH** (to the darkness):

Let’s burn through the plains.

---

**INT. MIDWEST CONNECT FLIGHT 312 – SOMEWHERE OVER MISSOURI – APRIL 4, 1992 – 8:12 A.M.**

The plane hums a soft, vibrating monotone.

Kathy’s asleep by the window, arms crossed, headphones half-fallen off her head.

She’s dreaming of nothing or everything—hard to say.

The band is scattered across rows, dead to the world.

Sarah is wide awake.

Tray table down.

Legal pad open.

A lukewarm airline coffee at her elbow and a mind in full campaign mode.

---

She writes at the top of a fresh page:

KANSAS CITY – Market Tactic Plan

* Radio: Mid-afternoon drive-time.

* College outreach: UMKC \+ KU satellite \+ zine collab in Westport.

* Signing: Vinyl Renaissance (target: 3–5 PM)

* Local press: Pitch Weekly lead, focus on Kathy’s lyrical ownership, less on “shock.”

* Street team? Check back with regional.

She flips the page.

---

OKLAHOMA CITY – Secondary Push / Personal Business

(pause, frowns slightly)

Personal Business.

She underlines it, then quickly draws a line through it again.

But her pen lingers.

---

**SARAH** (thinking):

Gilley.

Mercurial. Charming.

A walking distraction in a leather jacket and a smirk.

He lights up rooms and forgets their names on the way out.

And yet—

He’s sticky on FM radio.

He’s undeniable in-person.

He works on crowds.

And her.

And that’s the problem.

---

She flips to a clean sheet.

At the top, she writes:

Case Study: Can you market a man you want to fuck without looking like a bipolar nutjob?

(She circles it once. Then again.)

---

She writes underneath, methodical:

Pros:  
– Charisma \= transferable energy.  
– Sex appeal \= audience projection.  
– Strong regional pull in Southern/Midwest markets.  
– Useful for male demos that don’t “get” Kathy.  
Cons:  
– Unstable. Easily distracted.  
– Has definitely flirted with 3 interns. (Low estimate.)  
– Absolutely knows she wants him.  
– May weaponize it.  
– Cannot afford a breakdown or a workplace scandal.

---

She taps the pen on the tray.

Then jots one last line:

Strategy: Stay professional. Keep control. Market the band. Not the boy.

(long beat)

If he tries anything… let him.

(beat)

Just don’t let him know it worked.

---

The plane bumps.

Kathy stirs beside her.

KATHY (groggy):

Are we in Kansas yet?

---

**SARAH** (without looking up):

No. But I’ve already started the campaign.

---

KATHY (stretching):

You really don’t stop, huh?

---

**SARAH** (still writing):

Not until everyone knows your name.

And forgets his.

---

**INT. VINYL RENAISSANCE – KANSAS CITY, MISSOURI – APRIL 4, 1992 – 4:12 P.M.**

The shop is buzzing.

Smells like dust, vinyl sleeves, and leftover incense from whoever worked the morning shift.

Posters of Ride, PJ Harvey, and Björk compete for attention on the back wall, but today?

It’s Commuted Day.

A folding table is set up near the cassette racks.

The line snakes out the door and halfway down the sidewalk.

Most of them are girls. A few wide-eyed dudes. One pair of parents who definitely do not know what they’re about to buy.

---

Kathy is posted at the center of the table, Sharpie in hand, sleeves rolled up, flannel tied around her waist like a battle standard.

She’s grinning.

Actually grinning.

---

KATHY (to Sarah, between signatures):

I could do this all day.

(beat)

Beats working the bank drive-thru in winter with cracked knuckles and cold fingers.

---

**SARAH** (leaning behind her, clipboard in hand, smirking):

You saying you prefer chaos to customer service?

---

KATHY (signing a flannel sleeve, not even blinking):

I used to pass cigs in a change tray and dream about blowing the place up.

(grins wider)

Now I pass rage and therapy in plastic jewel cases.

(glancing up)

And people thank me for it.

---

A fan steps up, hands trembling, holds out a cracked cassette dub.

FAN (softly):

You made me write poetry for the first time in two years.

---

KATHY (without pause):

Then don’t stop.

(signs it “–K.E. / Keep spilling”)

---

**SARAH** (quietly, behind her):

You’re converting people.

---

KATHY:

Yeah.

(grins sideways at Sarah)

You’re the architect.

But I’m the altar.

---

They keep going.

Signatures. Notes. A few hugs.

A sharpie to a denim jacket that’ll never be washed again.

One older woman with streaks of silver hair asks if it’s too late to start over.

KATHY (signing her CD):

Not if you’ve still got breath and volume.

---

The line doesn’t slow.

Neither does the fire.

---

**INT. RENTAL CAR – OUTSKIRTS OF KANSAS CITY – APRIL 4, 1992 – 5:27 P.M.**

The signing is over.

The car is quiet except for the soft hum of the interstate and the occasional rattle of Sharpies in the glove box.

Kathy’s in the passenger seat, legs up on the dash, sipping ginger ale like it’s champagne.

Her boots are muddy. Her cheeks are still flushed from the high of fans calling her “the girl who gave them permission to feel.”

Sarah drives.

Window cracked. Hands tight on the wheel.

Neither of them speaks for a long beat.

Then—

---

KATHY (softly, looking out the window):

Parting’s such sweet sorrow, huh?

---

**SARAH** (nods, keeping her eyes on the road):

Yeah.

It’s been a good day.

(beat)

KC showed up.

---

KATHY (smirks):

I showed up.

(swigs her ginger ale)

You just made sure they knew what they were asking for.

---

**SARAH** (quietly):

That’s the job.

(beat)

That’s the deal.

---

They drive in silence for a moment longer.

Then Kathy says what’s been hanging in the air:

---

KATHY:

So.

Gilley’s flying west.

---

**SARAH** (nods slowly):

I know.

---

She doesn’t say it aloud, but her mind is already there:

Room reservation at the Sheraton in OKC—under her name.  
One queen bed.  
Standard booking.  
Nothing indulgent.  
Nothing that would draw attention.  
And she’s been thinking, just for a moment, that maybe she’d cancel it.  
That maybe she’d show up at Gilley’s hotel instead.

Because he’ll want her there.

And she might want to say yes.

But—

She doesn’t.

Because that paper trail?

It’s real.

---

Nick Stewart saw something.  
Caught a glance. A vibe. A pause that lingered.  
Chris Blackwell? She confessed before the deal inked.  
Because she couldn’t live with winning dirty—  
Not when she was playing this big.

---

**SARAH** (murmuring):

Too many people know.

(beat)

If this gets audited—

If expenses get questioned—

If I become a conflict of interest?

Then everything I built becomes his mistake instead of my legacy.

---

KATHY (without turning):

You’re not gonna go to him.

---

**SARAH** (flatly):

No.

(beat)

Not tonight.

---

KATHY (after a pause):

Doesn’t mean you don’t want to.

---

**SARAH** (quiet, steel in her voice):

Doesn’t mean I’ll let myself.

---

Kathy nods once.

And that’s all.

The car hums on through the Midwest dusk.

Kathy sipping quietly.

Sarah burning silently.

---

**INT. HOTEL – OKLAHOMA CITY – **SARAH**’S ROOM – APRIL 5, 1992 – 8:03 P.M.**

Sarah stands just inside the door.

She’s alone.

One overnight bag dropped by the dresser.

A thick manila folder full of press schedules, college radio contact lists, and Tower Records restock reports sits on the bed.

The room smells like neutral soap and corporate furniture.

The skyline flickers outside her window, all soft neon and flat plains, as if the city itself hasn’t decided whether it’s waking up or shutting down.

---

She peels off her blazer.

Unbuttons her collar.

Kicks off her boots with slow, precise movements.

Then sinks into the chair by the window, hands on the armrests like a queen in a plastic throne.

No wine. No music. Just plotting.

---

**SARAH** (murmuring to herself):

Alright, cowboy.

You want to be useful?

Let’s make it hurt.

---

She opens her notebook.

Flips to the page marked:

OKC Strategy – Phase II (w/Gilley Variable)

She writes beneath it in hard, controlled strokes:

He’s all balls and noise.  
Make it an asset. Not a liability.

---

**SARAH** (thinking):

He walks like he owns a jukebox.

Smiles like sin, talks like a bad idea, and pulls radio programmers into orbit without even trying.

Everywhere he goes, he’s noticed.

Not for his talent—\*for his presence.

He’s volume.

But volume can be tuned. Directed. Weaponized.

---

She jots down:

Tactic 1: Put him in front of male programmers.  
Tactic 2: Pair him w/ college girls w/ too many theories and not enough chaos.  
Tactic 3: Let him flirt w/ the enemy.  
Tactic 4: Keep him dumb. Let me drive.

---

She pauses.

Bites her pen cap.

Looks out the window again.

**SARAH** (softly):

You make noise, Gilley.

I make narratives.

(beat)

You swagger.

I convert.

(beat)

So tomorrow?

You’re the bait.

I’m the hook.

---

She flips to a fresh page.

Titles it:

“OKC: What I Want Them to Remember”

And begins writing.

Not about sex.

Not about him.

Not about feelings.

Just impact.

Control.

And conquest.

---

Sarah’s alone.

And utterly dangerous.

---

**INT. KOSU STUDIOS – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 6, 1992 – 11:04 A.M.**

The studio smells faintly of warm equipment, coffee that’s been reheated twice, and vinyl dust.

Local DJ Tad Dixon, thirty-five and jaded, hasn’t been rattled by much in the last ten years—until this morning.

He just played Next to the Ice Cream Shop live on air.

He’s still sweating.

---

TAD (live on mic, breathing shallow):

That was Commuted.

(pause)

Yeah, you heard that right.

Track’s called Next to the Ice Cream Shop.

(longer pause)

I don’t know what just hit me.

But I want to air it twice.

(flicks mic off, leans back)

Jesus H. Christ.

---

**INT. KOSU GREEN ROOM – MOMENTS LATER**

Gilley’s leaning against the counter, casually flipping through a local alt-weekly, wearing that same dangerous smile that could close a college bar and open three marriages.

Sarah sits at the edge of the couch, arms folded, watching the studio lights flicker.

Kathy’s still in transit. But this? This was Gilley’s test run.

And the DJ bit.

Hard.

---

TAD (walking in, flushed):

Okay.

So.

That track?

(pointing at Gilley)

You said it was autobiographical.

---

GILLEY (smirking):

I said it might be.

---

TAD:

Man… that’s the most erotically disturbing shit I’ve ever heard in 12 years of public radio.

(grabs a seat, still flustered)

What’s it about?

---

GILLEY (tilting his head, easy):

Tension.

Power.

Sensuality with… sharp corners.

(grins)

Or maybe just a really good massage.

---

**SARAH** (cutting in, sharp):

It’s a story.

Not a confession.

(beat)

We let the listener decide where the truth is.

(to Gilley)

Right?

---

GILLEY (not taking the bait):

Exactly.

(beat)

But if you ever had a night that ended in incense, bad decisions, and a locked door next to a Baskin-Robbins…

(shrugs)

You get it.

---

TAD (laughing, half in awe):

That track’s gonna haunt my call-ins for a week.

Your voice sounds like regret wrapped in velvet.

(beat)

What’s your favorite on the record?

---

GILLEY (without hesitation):

The Hilton?

(shakes his head)

Nah.

It’s this one.

Next to the Ice Cream Shop.

It’s weird.

It’s dirty.

It’s dangerous in a way you don’t understand until it’s too late.

(beat)

Kathy doesn’t just sing it. She summons it.

---

**SARAH** (quietly, to herself):

That’s why it works.

---

**INT. KOSU – STUDIO CONTROL ROOM – 11:47 A.M. – APRIL 6, 1992**

The phone bank is lit up like a Christmas tree dipped in kerosene.

Red lights blinking.

Line 3’s on hold.

Line 5 is furious.

Line 6? Breathless.

And Line 2?

Line 2 just keeps asking:

“Did she really say ‘the glans burst forth’ or did I hallucinate that?”

---

TAD is back on the air.

His sleeves rolled up.

Forehead glistening.

---

TAD (LIVE):

For those of you just tuning in, that was Commuted.

Yes, that Commuted.

Yes, the track was Next to the Ice Cream Shop.

And no, we are not sorry.

(glancing sideways)

You asked for authenticity.

You got a pagan prayer in 4/4 time.

---

INT. GREEN ROOM – CONTINUOUS

Sarah’s pacing.

Half-furious, half-exhilarated.

Gilley’s sprawled in a chair, arms wide, absolutely thriving.

---

GILLEY (grinning):

Thus according to prophecy.

---

**SARAH** (spinning on her heel):

You’re insufferable.

---

GILLEY (deadpan):

No, I’m a master-class storyteller.

(points at the phone bank)

That’s reaction.

That’s impact.

That’s sex and myth and a massage table from hell braided into poetry.

(beat)

You didn’t book me here because I’m subtle.

---

**SARAH** (through gritted teeth):

I booked you here to sell the record.

---

GILLEY (smirking):

And I just tripled demand.

You’re welcome.

---

TAD (stepping back in, a little wild-eyed):

We’ve got church ladies calling to ask what “asp massage” means.

(beat)

And one guy asking if Christine still takes clients.

---

GILLEY (chuckling):

Tell ’em the waiting list’s eternal.

(leans back)

But the conversion’s instant.

---

**SARAH** (muttering):

I swear to God, if you start believing your own myth…

---

GILLEY:

Too late.

(grins, eyes twinkling)

I am the myth.

---

**INT. HOTEL ROOM – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 6, 1992 – 11:33 P.M.**

The AC hums.

The curtains flutter against the cracked-open window.

The skyline flickers, low and distant.

Gilley is asleep.

Dead to the world.

Flat on his back, mouth slightly open, arm flopped above his head like he’s earned rest through pure charm.

Naked.

So is Sarah.

But she’s wide awake, sitting up in bed, knees pulled to her chest, the motel blanket tangled behind her.

The hotel clock radio plays the KOSU rebroadcast in low stereo hum.

It’s halfway through the second chorus of Next to the Ice Cream Shop.

Kathy’s voice floats across the room—slippery, half-lit, erotic, and ruthless.

“Poisoned promise, wrapped in silk…”

Sarah closes her eyes.

It lands differently now.

She picks up her notepad from the nightstand.

The one she keeps close.

The one with plans and strategy and damage control.

She flips to a blank page.

Writes:

He might be right.

(beat)

If true… then what?

---

She stares at it.

Pen still in hand.

Gilley exhales in his sleep beside her—some contented, stupid little sigh that drives her crazy in the worst way.

---

Sarah thinks:

He’s myth material.  
And I just slept with the press kit.  
Can I keep him useful without becoming disposable?  
Can I market him and not fall for him?  
Can I pull the strings and not get tangled in them?

---

She underlines it again:

Then what?

---

Because if he’s right—

If this is what the audience wants—

Not just Kathy’s fire or her strategy’s spark,

But Gilley’s instinct?

Then her rules don’t apply anymore.

And she’ll have to ask herself a different question:

What happens when the tool becomes the storyteller?

(beat)

And what happens when the woman holding the strings realizes she’s already tied to him?

---

She clicks off the radio.

Kathy’s final line echoes in her head anyway.

“In this poisoned promise, our souls entwined…”

---

Sarah leans back, naked, exhausted, eyes wide open.

And doesn’t sleep.

Not tonight.

---

**INT. HOTEL ROOM – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 7, 1992 – 6:48 A.M.**

The sun hasn’t fully risen.

The curtains glow faint orange.

A faint rumble of trucks outside hums against the windowpane.

Sarah is already dressed—blazer on, hair tied back, clipboard in hand. Her eyes are glassy, but her face is composed. She hasn’t slept. But you wouldn’t know it unless you looked too long.

Behind her, Gilley stirs beneath the sheets. He stretches like a jungle cat in satin sheets he doesn’t deserve.

He sits up, grinning like the cat who ate the A\&R girl.

---

GILLEY (rubbing his jaw):

Morning, boss lady.

(beat)

You sleep at all?

---

**SARAH** (without turning):

Plenty.

---

GILLEY (sly):

Liar.

(beat)

Anyway, I’ve got a brilliant idea.

You, me, Denny’s.

(he throws on his jeans without closing them)

I’m talking fake maple syrup, hash browns, and bottomless coffee until we float off the table.

Then you do whatever empire-building thing you’ve got on the schedule today, and—

(finger guns)

We find a pinball arcade.

---

**SARAH** (still writing):

I’m meeting KOSU’s program director at noon.

We have a record store push at 2:15.

Then a live Q\&A at the Oklahoma Civic Youth Center.

---

GILLEY (grinning):

Cool, cool.

(steps closer, voice low)

And after dinner?

(leans in)

I was thinking… ice cream.

(beat, smirking)

And eating you out.

---

Sarah doesn’t flinch.

Doesn’t pause her note-taking.

---

**SARAH** (even, sharp):

You have five minutes to shower and fifteen to be dressed.

(finally looks up)

If we’re late to KOSU, you’re buying more than breakfast.

---

GILLEY (hands raised, grinning wider):

God, you’re hot when you’re terrifying.

(grabs his shirt, heads for the bathroom)

Denny’s, a microphone, pinball, and sex?

(beat, muffled as he disappears)

This might be the greatest day of my life.

---

Sarah exhales.

Looks down at her notes.

Then slowly, on the margin of the itinerary, she writes:

He doesn’t know.  
He can’t.  
Not yet.

---

Then flips the page.

Because the grind waits.

And the ice cream promise?

It’ll keep.

---

**INT. KOSU – PROGRAM DIRECTOR’S OFFICE – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 7, 1992 – 12:03 P.M.**

The office is warm, filled with natural light and the smell of coffee and paper.

Framed posters of folk legends and jazz greats cover the walls, interrupted only by a whiteboard labeled “Rotation Priorities.”

Seated at the desk is Judy Morales, late 30s, sharp-eyed, effortlessly stylish, and very much in control of her domain.

She’s built KOSU into an eclectic powerhouse—part NPR, part outlaw signal—and she can smell ego, fear, or fake press packets a mile away.

Which is why she’s completely fascinated by Gilley.

He’s draped sideways in the guest chair, smiling like he owns the room, shirt unbuttoned just enough to toe the FCC line, a cup of coffee untouched in front of him.

Sarah sits beside him, posture immaculate, clipboard across her lap like a shield.

---

JUDY (leaning forward):

So let me ask you, Gilley—

(smiling, playful)

What’s it like working that close to Kathy Espich?

I mean… is she really as raw in real life as she sounds?

(eyes sparkling)

Or is that just part of the myth?

---

GILLEY (grinning)

Well, you know… Kathy’s like a thunderstorm.

(beat)

You don’t control it. You survive it.

---

JUDY (laughs, maybe a bit too long):

God, I love that.

(glancing at Sarah)

She lets you have space on the record?

---

GILLEY (leaning in):

She lets me sweat on the floor just enough to keep people guessing.

---

JUDY (smirking, biting her pen cap):

I’m guessing she knows exactly what you’re doing.

(eyes flicking to his shirt collar)

Do you usually show up to public radio looking like a roadie for INXS?

---

Sarah clears her throat.

Her pen taps once against her clipboard.

Sharp. Clean. Deliberate.

**SARAH** (with practiced warmth):

Gilley adds a little chaos to the campaign.

It makes Kathy’s precision stand out more.

(smiling slightly)

That contrast is intentional.

(turns to Judy)

But we’re here to talk rotation, right?

I’ve got three tracks queued that fit your late-night window perfectly.

And we’re pushing The Hilton and Next to the Ice Cream Shop for public response testing.

---

JUDY (sits up, just a little):

Right. Yes.

(clears her throat, adjusts posture)

I heard the KXMB reaction. It’s… insane.

But the lyrics? They’re riding that very fine line.

---

**SARAH** (nodding):

That’s why we’re testing with off-hours audiences first.

Let the listeners choose discomfort.

Let it resonate, not just echo.

(beat)

We’re not asking for a prime slot.

We’re asking for impact.

---

GILLEY (grinning, lazy):

Besides… the ones who hear it at midnight?

(beat)

They’re the ones who need it most.

---

JUDY (finally back on track, but still a little flustered):

Alright.

Give me the three-track sampler.

(beat)

And maybe… one quote from Kathy. Something I can use in the rotation blurbs.

---

**SARAH** (already flipping pages):

She said: “If you feel seen and scared, it’s working.”

---

JUDY (writing it down, nodding):

That’ll do.

(smiles at Gilley)

And I’ll try to avoid getting caught in the storm.

---

**SARAH** (gracious but firm):

If it’s Commuted, you’re already in it.

(beat)

Now let’s talk audience metrics.

---

**INT. GUESTROOM RECORDS – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 7, 1992 – 2:23 P.M.**

The store hums with low-grade chaos:

The dusty hum of amps.

The clatter of cassette boxes.

The pulse of Commuted’s The Hilton playing over the speakers, just loud enough to stir the hips of the patient and the possessed alike.

The signing table is a warzone of Sharpies, vinyl sleeves, and jittery hands.

Gilley sits at the center, loose and glowing, like he’s been sculpted out of sex appeal and bad intentions.

The line wraps halfway around the floor.

Everyone wants something signed.

CDs. Posters. Napkins. A girl’s torn-off denim jacket sleeve.

And then?

The first breast.

---

**SARAH** watches from the corner, arms folded, expression unreadable behind her well-practiced PR smile.

The girl is giggling.

Gilley is smooth.

He signs her with one hand and holds eye contact with the other.

**SARAH** (internally):

Goddamn it, Gilley…

---

By the fifth one, she’s ready to combust.

Until that fifth girl—mid-30s, quiet, scar along her clavicle, shirt lifted with slow dignity—leans in close and says something Sarah doesn’t catch.

Gilley pauses.

His grin fades into something gentler.

He signs carefully.

Just over the reconstructed curve.

The pen presses slow.

Intentional.

Then she smiles.

Not giggles. Not blushes.

Just… peace.

---

**SARAH** blinks.

Everything slows for a moment.

It wasn’t performance.

Not that time.

And maybe not the others, either.

---

**SARAH** (thinking):

It doesn’t matter who’s in the crowd.

He makes them feel like they matter most.

Like they weren’t just number 153 in line.

But the only person on Earth he wanted to see.

---

She watches the woman press her hand over the signature.

She says something to the staff—about turning the ink into a tattoo.

---

**SARAH** (quietly to herself):

That’s not just charisma.

That’s alchemy.

(beat)

He’s not playing them.

He’s lifting them.

---

She keeps watching.

Gilley laughs with someone’s dad.

Leans down to high-five a kid in oversized headphones.

Tells someone they’re braver than they think.

And in that moment—

Sarah’s not seething anymore.

She’s staggered.

---

**SARAH** (scribbling in her planner):

Asset, unpredictable.  
Dangerous, magnetic.  
But maybe… necessary.

---

**INT. MIDWEST ZINE PRINTING CO-OP – LAWRENCE, KANSAS – APRIL 10, 1992 – 1:39 P.M.**

The air is thick with toner fumes and stale coffee.

Stacks of folded pages are being collated by hand.

The newest issue of GIRLS WITHOUT BRAKES is coming off the Risograph printer one sticky, beautiful sheet at a time.

And on page seven, dead center in the fold, printed in stark black and white, is the photocopied close-up of Shannon’s tattooed breast:

A nude, reconstructed chest, proud and unfiltered.

The scar, visible.

The signature, sharp: “Jeremy Gilley.”

And beneath it, a hand-inked quote, in loopy, unmistakably female handwriting:

“She made me feel powerful in the parts I was told were ruined.”

---

The editors didn’t caption it.

Didn’t crop it.

Didn’t censor it.

Just printed it raw, centerfold-style.

And in the weeks that follow?

That page becomes holy.

---

The issue spreads like wildfire:

* Omaha punk girls start screenprinting it onto denim jackets.

* A tattoo shop in Des Moines puts it in their waiting room under a sign that reads, “Reclaim What Was Taken.”

* Columbia, Missouri radio hosts read the quote on-air and dedicate a week of programming to “Women Who Unbroke Themselves.”  
---

By April’s end, three states whisper the same name.

Not Kathy.

Not Commuted.

Not even Gilley.

“Shannon.”

---

They say it like legend.

Like proof.

Like “she went through it and came out wielding ink like a sword.”

---

And in zine circles, the image becomes folklore.

A breast.

A scar.

A signature.

A story that refused to ask permission.

---

**INT. OKLAHOMA CIVIC YOUTH CENTER – AUDITORIUM – APRIL 7, 1992 – 7:04 P.M.**

The room buzzes with the scent of sweat, denim, and cheap body spray.

Folding chairs creak. The lights hum. The Commuted logo is spray-painted across a bedsheet hanging behind the stage.

Sarah is stage-left, clipboard in hand, eyes sharp and roving, scanning the crowd like she’s counting variables at a lab experiment.

Kathy leans into her mic, relaxed but surgical, answering a question about lyrical risk and voice with that cool, ragged tone that makes a girl cry and a boy second-guess his major.

Gilley sits beside her, legs wide, arms draped over the back of his chair like the local god of relaxed danger.

The audience is 70% college guys—frat types, flannel punks, the occasional stoned poet.

They’re boisterous, brash, and riding the edge between infatuation and intimidation.

---

Q\&A MODERATOR (calling on next question):

Back left—yeah, you in the Ball State hoodie.

---

DUDE \#1 (loud, smirking):

Yo, Gilley\!

(half the crowd chuckles, waiting for the jab)

Why the hell do you let Jeremy Evans roll all over you on guitar?

(beat)

Every time he solos, you just fade into the wallpaper.

(scattered oooohs from the peanut gallery)

---

Gilley leans forward slowly, grinning like he just found a snake in his boot and plans to name it.

GILLEY:

Let me tell you something, man.

(beat)

Jeremy doesn’t roll over me.

He flies.

And I ride the air current.

---

The room goes still for half a second.

Then one long “Damn…” from the front row.

---

GILLEY (continuing, unbothered):

If you wanna run the spotlight every second,

you end up burnt and alone.

(beat)

But if you know when to pull back and let the blade flash?

That’s how you cut deep.

(grins)

You don’t gotta scream to be dangerous.

---

Kathy (without missing a beat):

That’s the smartest thing he’s said all week.

---

DUDE \#1 (nodding, a little sheepishly now):

Okay.

Respect.

---

Another hand goes up.

Another boy with a challenge behind his question.

And Sarah watches it all.

She sees the envy.

The way they want to be Gilley.

Or beat Gilley.

But none of them understand that he wins by choosing when not to fight.

---

**SARAH** (to herself, quietly):

He doesn’t need to dominate the track.

He haunts it.

---

She marks something on her clipboard.

New angle:  
Gilley \= restraint.  
Jeremy \= combustion.  
The band is not a competition. It’s a ritual.  
Sell that.

---

**INT. OKLAHOMA CIVIC YOUTH CENTER – HALLWAY OUTSIDE THE AUDITORIUM – APRIL 7, 1992 – 8:02 P.M.**

The Q\&A is over. The crowd is thinning.

Most of the guys are wandering out, buzzed from proximity to Commuted like they just touched a live wire.

But one freshman guitarist—blond, skinny, flannel tied around his waist, notebook full of quotes and half-formed dreams—hangs back by the edge of the stage.

He approaches Gilley, wide-eyed, reverent, starstruck in the kind of way that’s still pure.

---

FRESHMAN GUITARIST:

Dude… seriously?

(holding his notebook up)

You’re on this wild record, and you’re talking about taking a back seat?

(beat)

That’s what you want people to hear?

---

GILLEY (grinning, amused):

Yeah, man.

You know what rhythm guitar is?

(leans in, voice low and confident)

It’s gravity.

I’m the architecture holding the chaos in place.

(beat)

I share sonically.

I carry weight quietly.

(smirks)

Also, I sing a lot of backup.

---

FRESHMAN GUITARIST (still confused):

But… I mean, shouldn’t you want to stand out?

---

GILLEY (serious now):

Kid, if every musician starts out trying to dominate instead of contribute?

You’re not building a band.

(beat)

You’re building a shitload of solo acts who can’t keep a drummer past two gigs.

(deadpan)

We call that masturbation in the industry.

---

**SARAH** (behind him, stage-whispering):

No, we don’t.

---

GILLEY (without turning):

I will make it true by the time we get to Dallas.

(beat, then brighter)

That’s branding, sweetheart.

---

The freshman laughs—genuine, floored.

He jots it down in big letters:

“Contribute. Don’t dominate.  
Rhythm \= Gravity.  
Solo \= Masturbation.”

---

**KATHY reappears from the side door, water bottle in hand, sweat still clinging to her collarbones, towel over her shoulder.**

**GILLEY (turning to her, grinning):**

**And here comes my partner in crime.**

**(louder, to the crowd still trickling out)**

**She’s awesome, dudes.**

---

**KATHY (dry):**

**Why do I feel like I’m about to be held legally responsible for whatever you just said?**

---

**GILLEY (raising both hands like a preacher):**

**Because you’re with me again.**

**(beat, quieter, warm)**

**Welcome back to the grind.**

---

**KATHY (half-smiling):**

**Let’s wreck another city.**

---

**The freshman watches them, starstruck.**

**Not by celebrity.**

**But by the way they orbit each other.**

---

**INT. PARKING LOT OUTSIDE OKLAHOMA CIVIC YOUTH CENTER – APRIL 7, 1992 – 8:31 P.M.**

The night’s cooling off.

The streetlamps hum.

Amplifier buzz still hangs in the air like leftover lightning.

Sarah’s tucking her clipboard under her arm, half-dialed into logistics for the next day’s station drop.

She’s already in post-game mode—voice tight, eyes scanning like she’s still running perimeter.

Gilley’s bouncing on his heels like a kid with change in his pocket and nothing to lose.

Kathy leaned up against the rental van, sipping from a lukewarm water bottle and wiping the sweat from her neck with a merch rag.

---

GILLEY (grinning like he’s twelve and about to suggest mischief):

Alright. Listen.

Ice cream.

Then pinball.

(beat, turning to Kathy)

You in?

---

KATHY (raises an eyebrow):

This is your big plan?

Post-feminist sonic devastation followed by mint chocolate chip and flippers?

---

GILLEY (arms wide):

Hell yeah.

(grinning)

Come on, it’s America, baby—sugar and hand-eye coordination\!

---

**SARAH** (not even looking up from her planner):

And exactly four hours of freedom before the Tulsa wake-up call.

(finally glances up)

So pick wisely.

---

KATHY (pretending to groan):

Y’all are turning into wholesome.

---

GILLEY (mock offended):

Hey now.

There’s nothing wholesome about the way I eat ice cream.

(to Sarah, deadpan)

Back me up.

---

**SARAH** (without missing a beat):

You’re a menace to dessert.

---

GILLEY (grinning at Kathy):

So?

You in, partner-in-crime?

Let’s wreck a pinball machine or two before we go back to wrecking cities.

---

KATHY (beat, then smirks):

Only if I get first shot at Attack from Mars.

And I’m not sharing a sundae.

---

GILLEY (bowing low):

It would be my honor.

---

**SARAH** (quiet, smiling to herself):

This is gonna be the most dangerous sugar rush in Oklahoma history.

---

They walk off together, the three of them—

a war strategist, a myth in boots, and the smirking charm grenade between them.

Their battlefields would wait.

Tonight?

There was ice cream and pinball.

---

**INT. THE QUARTER ZONE ARCADE – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 7, 1992 – 9:08 P.M.**

The air is heavy with neon and sugar, blinking machines, and the chirping soundtrack of a hundred childhoods never quite outgrown.

Carpet’s sticky.

Change machines are half-busted.

The lighting? Aggressively nostalgic.

“Attack from Mars” blinks like a taunt in the back corner.

Kathy’s already elbows-deep in it, flannel sleeves pushed up, cigarette behind her ear even though there’s a strict no-smoking policy.

She’s leaning in, focused like she’s negotiating the nuclear codes with flippers and spite.

Her score?

Already doubling the previous high.

A kid in a trench coat watches from a safe distance, awestruck.

---

**SARAH** is off to the side, arms folded, still holding a melting swirl cone she’s forgotten to eat.

She’s not watching the machine.

She’s watching Gilley.

---

He’s leaning on the prize counter, chatting up the girl behind it—Abby, early 20s, green hair under a visor, surrounded by shelves of plastic kazoos and inflatable hammers.

Gilley (grinning, voice low and warm):

You ever think about just walking out of here with the lava lamp and living your best life?

---

ABBY (laughing, eyes sparkling):

You gonna win me the tickets for it?

---

GILLEY (mock offended):

Girl, I won your attention and that’s priceless.

---

**SARAH** (to herself, flat):

He’s using a lava lamp as a pickup line.

(beat)

I hate how well it’s working.

---

She watches him hand over his ridiculous stash of tickets—won from Skee-Ball and a basketball shooter he took way too seriously—and watches Abby slide a rubber monster finger across the counter to him in exchange.

He puts it on.

Gilley (to Sarah, holding it up like Excalibur):

Fear me, I’m armed\!

---

**SARAH** (dryly):

Congratulations. You’re five.

---

Back by the machine, Kathy smacks the side of the cabinet like it owes her money.

KATHY (to no one):

That’s right.

High score, bitches.

---

KID IN TRENCH COAT (awed):

You’re like… a pinball priestess.

---

KATHY (grinning):

Worship at the flippers and bring me Dippin’ Dots.

---

Gilley saunters over, monster finger still on.

GILLEY:

I got a finger, she got high score, you’ve got a clipboard in your brain—

(to Sarah)

—can we call this night complete?

---

**SARAH** (finally taking a bite of her cone):

Almost.

(beat, looking at both of them)

But tomorrow?

We’re back to conquest.

---

KATHY:

Then tonight?

We tilt.

---

They laugh.

Not strategy.

Not seduction.

Not survival.

Just joy.

---

**EXT. STRIP MALL PARKING LOT – OKLAHOMA CITY – APRIL 7, 1992 – 9:52 P.M.**

The night is cool and dry.

Streetlights buzz above like drunk angels.

The Quarter Zone Arcade glows behind them in flickering red neon.

Sarah, Kathy, and Gilley stroll across the cracked pavement toward the Commuted van, the kind that still smells faintly like amps and fries from three cities ago.

---

Kathy’s got a styrofoam cup of Dippin’ Dots, half-melted.

She’s licking cherry red off her knuckles, walking with the supreme satisfaction of someone who just ruled the realm of flippers and flashing lights.

---

KATHY (to Sarah):

Next city?

You’re buying me two rolls of quarters and a milkshake.

(beat)

I own Skee-Ball now.

---

**SARAH** (deadpan, licking her cone):

We’ll add it to the rider.

---

And then—

a sound cuts through the parking lot.

A tight, raw blast of bluesy harmonica.

Fast. Dirty. Unapologetic.

“Whammer Jammer.”

---

**SARAH** (startled, whirls around):

Wait—what the hell?

---

GILLEY stands ten feet back, blowing into a shiny, cheap harmonica, full mouth-to-metal swagger, body grooving like he’s channeling every Midwestern roadhouse ghost in a two-mile radius.

---

KATHY (mouth full of dots, stunned):

You can play?

---

GILLEY (pulls it away, grinning):

Don’t look so shocked.

(beat, proudly)

I bought it with your tickets.

(spins it like a six-shooter)

Consider it tribute to the queen of the tilt.

---

KATHY (half-laughing):

I thought you were just blowing hot air as usual.

---

**SARAH** (under her breath):

Apparently, some of it’s musical.

---

GILLEY:

I’ve got layers.

(beat, winking)

Like an onion made of charisma.

---

He launches back in—quick riffs, dancing across the notes with grit and glee, while Sarah and Kathy just stand there, half-amused, half-impressed.

Because they didn’t know.

And now they do.

---

As he winds down, he ends on a flourish, bows deeply in the parking lot light.
